# Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_137 messages · 19 participants · 2003-02_

### Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-07T21:00:53+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net>`

```
Best Practices - Cobol
Robert Wagner
[saved as plain text]

Introduction

Teaching someone the grammar of a programming language and expecting
well-written code is like teaching someone to type and expecting
literature. Neophyte programmers all make the same mistakes. Intuition
and common sense are not adequate to write quality code. I spent many
years learning to write Cobol well. This book will teach you to get it
right the first time, enabling you to bypass the learning curve. Over
its history, the Cobol language has accumulated many features that are
Bad Practices and/or obsolete. Encyclopedic reference books describe
them all in alphabetical order without giving the reader a clue as to
which are good, bad and superfluous. Rather than compiling a list of
negatives.. don't do this and that .. I simply omitted the bad
features. The syntax in the first three chapters is all you need to
write the most complex programs. Much of this tutorial is in the
samples and Demo program, not the descriptive text.

About Compilers

A frequently asked question in Cobol newsgroups and Web forums
is 'Where can I get a free or inexpensive compiler?' The answer is
Fujitsu, which offers a full-featured, 32-bit, 'educational' compiler
for free download from http://www.adtools.com/student/description.htm
I developed the Demo program using this compiler. If you want
documentation with your compiler, buy the book Sam's Teach Yourself
Cobol in 24 Hours by Thane Hubbell for about $30. It comes with the
same compiler plus a 60-day trial of sp2, a wonderful GUI tool, plus
Fujitsu documentation in pdf files. The CD contains another set of
softcopy documentation which cannot be read because the included
Common Ground reader is corrupt. The book you're reading now is all
you really need.

Be careful to get a 32-bit compiler rather than a 16-bit DOS-era one.
Personal Micro Focus compilers are all 16-bit, which they try to
disguise by saying "Compatible with Windows and NT" or "For Windows."
Sure, because Microsoft operating systems are backward compatible with
16-bit applications. Also, be sure to get a compiler that produces
native machine code rather than interpreted pseudo-code (p-code).
Personal Micro Focus is interpreted. If you write Cobol at work, your
employer will provide the compiler. Even in that case, you'll want a
compatible compiler at home for tinkering with job-related projects.
You can insure compatibility by sticking to the 1985 language
Standard, with the addition of pointers. The next language spec will
be called the 2002 Standard.

The best compilers are:

Micro Focus

The de-facto leader. Runs primarily on Unix and PC. The company offers
no inexpensive 32-bit, native code versions. Sells for $2-4K.

IBM

The other gold standard. Runs primarily on mainframes. Visual Age for
Windows, the PC version, sells for $3K.

Fujitsu

A good compiler, whose weaknesses are no documentation of system
calls, incorrect documentation of pointer operations, a poor workbench
and some bugs, as we will see in the demo program. The current release
sells for $800, which some will find affordable. The free version is
almost as good.

Also-rans are:

CA Realia

In its day (late 80's), the best compiler written .. any language, any
machine. The compiler was written in Cobol. Since Computer Associates
acquired the company, the compiler has been neglected and fallen
behind. Also, it's expensive.

Kobol

From the Open Source folks (BSD, GNU, Linux), although source is not
open and it sells for $50. Written by C programmers with disdain for
Cobol. It lacks free-format, thus is not compliant with the 1985 and
2002 Standards. It lacks pointers, making it incompatible with
mainstream compilers above. Expect other incompatibilities due to C
programmers' bad attitude toward Cobol.

Cobol 650

A free, homegrown, buggy 16-bit DOS compiler.

Acucobol

Uses much non-standard syntax in order to provide GUI and Web
capabilities. Programs developed under Acu will not compile under an
ANSI-standard compiler. There are better ways to get GUI and Web
capabilities. Acu is a p-code interpreter.

Liant RM

Produces interpreted p-code, which means slow execution. Has not been
enhanced in ten years.

LegacyJ PERCobol

Produces code for the Java Virtual Machine, which is a slow p-code
interpreter.

The future of Cobol

The hottest topic in Cobol now (2003) is compilers for dot-NET. Both
Micro Focus and Fujitsu offer them, priced around $4K. The advantages
of dot-NET are interoperability between languages, security and
ability to run on any machine without recompiling. The disadvantages
are that you're not allowed to use pointers and it is proprietary to
Microsoft. OLE/COM gave us interoperability. So did CORBA and even
XML, each in its own way. Problem is, Cobol programmers chose to
ignore those interfaces. Dot-NET portends to make it impossible for
Cobol programmers to ignore the non-Cobol world. That's good, but not
at the price of being locked into Microsoft.

The previous hottest topic was Object Oriented (OO) Cobol, which has
generated much discussion but little usable code. The problem with OO
Cobol is verbosity. Rewriting a traditional Cobol program into OO
Cobol produces 3-5 times as many lines of code. Other languages do not
impose that penalty. Rewriting C to C++ produces about the same line
count. Cobol has always been denounced for being too verbose. It was
untrue for traditional Cobol, as you're about to see, but OO Cobol
really is too verbose. Moreover, it doesn't have the flavor of Cobol
nor of OO. Its grammar was poorly designed.


Table of Contents
1. Basics, divisions and sections
2. Basics, data types and structures
3. Basics, procedural verbs
4. Programming style
5. Logic, if and when
6. Procedural structure, call
7. Data structure, linked lists
8. Data structure, files and database
9. Lookup
10. Sorting
11. Graphic User Interface (GUI)
12. Demo program

Chapter 1

Basics, divisions and sections

A minimal program, which compiles without warnings and runs correctly
using Micro Focus:

procedure division.
display 'hello world'.

Header model:

data division.
working-storage section.             *> local variables
01  unqualified-variables.
    05  variable-name       comp-5  pic s9(09).
linkage section.
 01  structure-name.                 *> distant variables
     05  field-name                 pic  x(30).
procedure division using structure-name.
main.
        move 'hello' to field-name
        goback.

Historically, Cobol had four divisions: identification, environment,
data and procedure. Today, the number has been reduced to two: data
and procedure, although environment is still necessary if the program
accesses files because it contains the SELECT statements (described in
chapter 8). Identification division was always comments only; it is no
longer used.

The data division has three sections-file, working-storage and
linkage-in which you describe data structures. All three follow the
same format, the difference being where the data is located in memory.
File and working-storage contain structures inside the program's local
memory; linkage section contains structures outside the program-either
passed by a calling program or dynamically allocated. The procedure
division, of course, contains your procedural code.

Historically, Cobol was stored on punched cards and had rules saying
columns 1-6 contained a sequence number, so you could sort the cards
in case you dropped the deck, column 7 was where you put an asterisk
indicating the line contained comments, columns 8-72 contained your
program text, columns 73-80 was a comment area usually used for
version control. Programs were written in upper case. These formatting
rules went away eighteen years ago, in 1985, but mainframe programmers
continue to use the obsolete format. Position 1 is now for the
asterisk formerly in column 7, line position 2 through a very big
limit contains your program text. You can write comments to the right
of program text by using *>. Best Practice: use lower case and "free
form", but avoid creating lines (much) longer than your text editor
can display without scrolling.

Every compiler comes with a 'workbench' that includes a text editor.
You are not required to use the supplied editor. You may use any tool
that can edit an ASCII text file, for example Wordpad, MS DevStudio or
emacs. It is not necessary to learn a new editor for each compiler.

Chapter 2

Basics, data types and structures

Strings:

01  a-string                           pic  x(30).     *> string
01  b-string                           pic  x(70).     *> string

A Cobol string is fixed length, filled with spaces on the right. When
you move a string to another of different length, it is truncated if
the receiving field is smaller, padded with spaces if the receiving
field is larger. Thus, "move a-string to b-string" copies 30 bytes and
fills with 40 spaces.

Cobol can handle null-terminated C-style strings.
01  c-string                            pic  x(31).     *> C-style string
01  cobol-string                      pic  x(30).

Convert C to Cobol
move spaces to cobol-string
unstring c-string delimited by low-value into cobol-string

Convert Cobol to C
string cobol-string delimited by '  '  low-value into c-string
*> look for 2 spaces

.. and Pascal-style strings with an explicit length.
    05  pascal-structure.     *> pascal-style string
        10  string-length   comp-5  pic s9(04).
        10  pascal-string.
            15  filler occurs 1 to 30 depending on string-length pic x.
     05  cobol-string               pic  x(30).

Convert Pascal to Cobol
move pascal-string to cobol-string

Convert Cobol to Pascal
move zero to string-length
inspect cobol-string tallying string-length for characters before initial  '  '
move cobol-string to pascal-string

Structures:
01  structure-name.
    05  smaller-structure.
        10  element-1                   pic ...
        10  element-2                   pic ...
    05  next-structure.
        10  element-1                   pic ...

The rules for moving structure names are the same as
strings-truncation when smaller, space-fill when larger.

Cobol lets you describe multi-dimensional records and structures in
the order of the bytes, which makes the code easy to understand. Other
languages, such as C, make you describe the innermost layers first,
then work your way out to the outermost layer. Most people find this
counter-intuitive and hard to visualize. As a result, they write
'flat' structures. In addition, Cobol offers superior qualification
syntax, in contrast to other languages which punish you for writing
multi-dimensional structures by requiring the 'full path' on every
reference, even when the field name is unique. Cobol requires only
enough qualification to make the reference unambiguous, for example
'element-1 in smaller-structure'. Not only is Cobol less verbose, it
encourages the good programming practice of organizing data into
hierarchies.

Numbers:
01  an-integer           comp-5  pic s9(09).     *> four bytes, 32 bits
01  half-word            comp-5  pic s9(04).     *> two bytes, 16 bits
01  quarter-word         comp-5  pic s9(02).     *> one byte, 8 bits
01  long-integer         comp-5  pic s9(15).     *> eight bytes, 64bits

These are integers stored in the machine's natural, highest-speed,
format. Deleting the sign or changing the number of digits will cause
additional overhead, which negates the reason for using this type. You
will use this format often for internal accumulators, but do not store
them in a file. Why? Because some machines are small-endian and some
are big-endian, which makes binary numbers non-portable between
machines. Also, it is more difficult to view files containing binary
numbers using general-purpose tools such as text editors, and
conversions such as EBCDIC-ASCII mangle them.

Although a 16-bit word can hold values to 32,767, your Cobol compiler
may not allow values greater than 9,999 because pic 9(04) means four
decimal digits.

01  display-number                   pic s9(08)v9(07).       *> display
01  unsigned-display-number          pic   9(05).

All numbers stored in files should be this type. The number is in
ASCII characters. Each digit occupies one byte. The sign is over the
low-order digit as 'A' for +1 and 'J' for -1. The v does not occupy a
byte of memory, it merely tells the compiler how to scale numbers.

The size limit of numbers is 15 digits plus a sign.
That will increase to 31 digits in the Cobol 2002 spec.

     05  high-speed-pennies                  comp-5  pic s9(15).
     05  display-pennies                             pic s9(15).
     05  display-dollars redefines display-pennies   pic s9(13)v9(02).

     move high-speed-pennies to display-pennies
     move display-dollars to edited-dollars

If you need only dollars and cents, define penny counters as integers.
When writing to a report use redefine to rescale, which is faster than
arithmetically dividing by 100.

01  packed-decimal-number      comp-3  pic s9(08)v9(07).

Packed is an obsolete format where each digit and the sign occupy half
a byte, called a nybble (cute, huh?). Historically, operations on
packed numbers were faster than on display because machines offered
hardware support. Modern machines do not, so there is no reason to use
packed numbers. Don't write these to a file because non-Cobol
languages do not natively support this format.

Indicators (flags):

     05  filler                           pic  x(01).
         88  end-of-input value 'e' false low-value.     *> named value
     set end-of-input to false
     if  end-of-input ...

There is usually no reason to give the indicator a name, and doing so
can make your code confusing. For example, moving 'e' to
end-of-input-flag, then testing it with the 88 name creates two names
for the same byte, which defeats the purpose of using 88. False works
only on Micro Focus; the others make you write another 88 for the
false value.

Pointers:

01  a-pointer                   *> pointer.
linkage section.
01  foo.
set a-pointer to address of foo
set address of foo to a-pointer

Pointers contain memory addresses, which are 32-bit. They are used
primarily to address structures in the linkage section, outside your
program's local memory. These structures can be dynamically allocated
or passed to you as parameters. Parameters don't need the explicit
syntax above, they are bound to structures by listing them in the
procedure division header.

Edited:
01  edited-number              pic zzz,zzz.99-.        *> zero suppress
01  edited -number             pic ---,---.99. *> single leading hyphen
move display-number to edited-number
compute edited-number rounded = (numerator * 100) / denominator

Arrays:

01  simple-array.
    05  array-element occurs 100        pic  x(10).     *> occurs

01  array-values.
    05  filler value '0 Sunday'         pic  x(10).     *> value
    05  filler value '1 Monday'         pic  x(10).
                ...
    05  filler value '6 Saturday'       pic  x(10).
01  searchable-array redefines array-values.            *> redefines
    05  array-element indexed x-array ascending-key antecedent occurs 7.
        10  antecedent                  pic  9(01).     *> key for SEARCH ALL
        10  filler                      pic  x(01).
        10  consequent                  pic  x(08).

See pascal-structure above for a variable length array. Indexes, such
as x-arrray, are useful only for searching. Historically, they were
provided as a high-speed alternative to subscripts. Nowadays, linked
lists, used extensively in the Demo, are faster and more flexible.

Floating-point:

01  a-float                        comp-1.
01  a-double                       comp-2.

Format is ANSI-standard, compatible with other programming languages.
Floating-point is seldom used by Cobol programmers.

Chapter 3

Basics, procedural verbs

move a to b                              *> all data types
move 123 to n                            *> move to number or edited
move consequent (da-of-week) to day-name *> using a subscript
move zero to b, c, d                     *> multiple receiving fields
move 'a value' to a                      *> will be space filled
move spaces to a, group-name             *> strings only
move low-values to group-name            *> binary zeros, strings only
move high-values to a                    *> FF, strings only
move x'0C' to a                          *> hex literal, strings only, will be
space filled
move all  x'20' to a                     *> all for repeating string literals

Copy data to another memory location. Doesn't 'move' the data, it is
still at the source location.

compute a = (b * c) / d         *> receiving field must be number or edited
compute p rounded = (n * 100) / d
compute b = a                   *> same as move a to b

add 1 to n
subtract b from c
multiply b by c
divide b into c
divide 7 into century-date giving week-number remainder da-of-week

Note that the receiving field is right-most word in all cases. Cobol
offers other syntax that reverses word order. Use these forms only.
Generally, compute is preferred over elemental arithmetic with the
exception of 'add 1', simple addition to an accumulator and cases
where you need the remainder. Compare Cobol's 'add a-number to total'
to other languages' 'total = total + a-number'. Which is more verbose?

set a-pointer to address of a-structure
set address of a-structure to a-pointer
set an-indicator to true
set an-index to 1

Set assigns values to pointers, internal pointers to structures,
indicators and index types.

if  a equal to b
        (do something)
end-if

if  a = b
    (do something)
    if  c = d
        (do something special)
    end-if
    (do something in either case)
else
    (do something else)
end-if

If is the most important 'verb' in Cobol, and the one which gives
beginners the most trouble. Chapter 5 covers conditionals in more
detail.

evaluate da-of-week
    when 0 move 'Sunday' to day-name
    when 1 move 'Monday' to day-name
        ..
    when other move '?' to day-name
end-evaluate

evaluate true
    when a = b and c = d
        (do something)
    when e > f
        (do something else)
end-evaluate

Evaluate is Cobol's 'case statement'. It is used in place of if when
there is more than one condition. Using it avoids inappropriate
nesting of ifs. For example, the second evaluate could be written as:

if  a = b and c = d
    (do something)
else
    if  e > f
        (do something else)
    end-if
end-if

This is awkward syntax. The second condition is not subordinate to the
first, it is on the same level. Other languages try to solve the
syntactical problem with a lexical solution, by inventing a word
"elseif", or a formatting solution "else if", in the hope no one will
notice two words on a line. The correct solution is a case statement
enabling you to test multiple conditions at the same level and
stopping after the first one found. The C language doesn't stop; it
keeps evaluating, which has caused many program bugs. Nested ifs are
error-prone because accidentally omitting an end-if will cause
undesired subordination of all code following.

perform varying n from 1 by 1 until     *> in-line loop
    n greater than 7 or antecedent (n) equal to da-of-week
    continue
end-perform

set end-of-input to false
perform read-input                      *> out-of-line call
perform until end-of-input
    perform one-record
    perform read-input
end-perform

perform read-input 2 times              *> times option

There are two types of perform: in-line loop control and a 'call' to
another paragraph. In the old days, calling another paragraph was the
only form. Nowadays, loop control is the predominant use. Using
perform to 'call' a paragraph is weak, like Basic's GOSUB, because it
doesn't permit you to pass parameters. It is preferable to use the
call verb to call local functions.

call 'program' using a, b, c

Passing control and parameters to another program, statically or
dynamically linked, written in Cobol or another language. Can also be
used to call a local function, similar to perform with the addition of
parameters. Chapter 6 and the Demo show how to do this.

move spaces to a-string
string 'Error ' error-code delimited by size into a-string *> compose 'Error xx'
string city delimited by '  ' ', ' state space zip delimited by size
into a-string
*> the above delimiter is two spaces
string cobol-string delimited by '  ', low-value into c-string

move spaces to input-words
unstring input-line delimited by all space into
    input-word (1) input-word (2) input-word (3) ...
*> all space means multiple spaces are treated as one delimiter

Neither string nor unstring fills its output with spaces; you must
initialize.

Unstring lacks power. For instance, it cannot preserve quoted
sub-strings. For non-trivial use, you'll wind up writing your own
parser, as I did in the Demo Program. Unstring is useful for
converting a null-terminated C-style string to a space-filled
Cobol-style string.

String is useful for building error messages, C-style strings and
simple output fields where variable-length elements are packed
together.

inspect a-string converting
    'ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ' to
    'abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz'

Inspect is an overly complex verb which isn't particularly useful.
You can program its functions yourself with more confidence they will
work as desired. It has one useful function: translating one character
set to another. On older compilers, this function was the Transform verb.

goback

This is how you exit, returning to the calling program or operating
system. Goback replaces 'stop run' and 'exit program'.

That's the essential kernel of the Cobol language: move, compute, set,
if, evaluate, perform and call. With these skills, you can program
anything except file IO, which you might never need. Nowadays most
file IO is done via databases, which are accessed via a call statement
rather than Cobol file syntax. I will cover file IO in chapter 8, the
search and sort verbs in chapters 9 and 10. The latter are convenience
features. You can write your own sorting and lookups using the above
syntax.

Chapter 4

Programming Style

* Learn to type. The leading cause of bad programming is fear of
having to type too much. If you plan to spend your life writing source
code on a keyboard, it seems reasonable to ask that you know how to
type. Nobody would hire a secretary lacking the skill, yet they hire
two-fingered programmers for a lot more money. Cobol has a reputation
for being 'too verbose'. That's a bum rap. It's no more verbose than
C, Java or Basic. But the mere accusation is sufficient to scare off
programmers insecure about their typing skills. The size difference
between high-quality and mediocre code is fully 50%. The way to avoid
typing is clean design, not abbreviations and short cuts.

* Learn logic. The second leading cause of bad programming is fear of
conditional statements the programmer finds 'too complex' to
understand. Chapter 5 will teach you logic.

* Write a procedure like a story -- with a beginning, middle and end.
In the beginning you clear counters, acquire memory, construct
objects, open files, etc. In the middle, you loop through whatever
you're doing. In the end you print out totals, release memory, destroy
objects, close files, etc. Initialization means 'done at the
beginning'. Programs that 'clean up' at the end of a procedure rather
than the beginning are poorly designed. As a formatting convention, I
leave a blank line between the beginning, middle and end.

*  Design telescopes. The middle of a procedure calls another procedure with a
beginning, middle and end.

a. open ....        perform until ....        call 'b' ....   close
b.            open ...  perform until ... call 'c' ...  close
c.                move zero .. perform d until ..
d.                      do something

The Demo program is a good example of telescoping design.

* Design from the top down; program from the bottom up. When you are
writing a program, part of the time you will be designing and part of
the time you will be programming. Learn to tell the difference so you
can employ the correct mental process. Mediocre programmers do
everything bottom-up.

* Use the company as a design model for your program or system. High
level code should make high level decisions; low level code should do
the grunt work and refer exceptions to higher levels for handling. If
the first code in your program opens files, you have the model
upside-down, you have a file clerk running the company. If low level
code can abort the run due to an exception, that's like giving a file
clerk authority to declare the company bankrupt. For real-time
applications, use the network model, in which modules pass messages to
each other in response to events. Think of each module as a company
department, within which there can still be a hierarchy.

* Write functions of reasonable size and simplicity. Glue them
together to form 'programs'. A reasonable size is 25-100 lines of
procedure. File IO is an especially good candidate for a callable
function. The Demo has five non-trivial functions within a single
program.

* Use descriptive data and procedure names that anyone can understand,
not abbreviations or jargon. Run a spell checker on your program. A
program is written once; it is read hundreds of times. If the purpose
of some code or data is not obvious, explain it with a comment written
in complete sentences.

* Pass data between programs in memory, not by writing it to a
temporary file. Files are for transport through time, not between
programs. Top down design makes this easy; bottom up design creates
temporary files.

* Handle errors. Do not ignore return codes. Do not say you'll get
around to it later.

* Check limits on arrays, pointers for null, loops for progress. Your
programs should never abend nor go into an endless loop.

* Write one statement per line. Indent four spaces under data
structures, if, evacuate, and perform.

* Use the free-form option of your compiler and write in lower case.

* Use no periods in procedural code. Missing and extra periods are a
common cause of program bugs. Exception: the compiler will insist on a
period terminating the last statement in a paragraph.

* Write in a consistent style in order to facilitate searching source
code. For example, when you want to search 25 source modules to find
every place where foo is modified, you can search on 'to foo'. That
search will not find 'to foo', 'compute foo = ' or 'giving foo'. If
your style is inconsistent, you will have to search on 'foo', then
waste time skipping references which do not modify it.

* Use linked lists rather than arrays. Lists use only the memory
required yet are nearly unlimited in size. Arrays wastefully allocate
the worst case, then are limited to that size. Moreover, stepping
through a list is faster than stepping through an array because the
compiler must do a multiplication on every subscripted reference.
Never store an array in a file. Files should be designed with one
record per occurrence, which is the standard design model for
databases.

* Use explicit parentheses when a statement contains both (AND and OR)
or ( (+ or -) and (* or /)). The compiler has 'precedence rules' to
disambiguate statements lacking parentheses. Don't learn them, lest
you be tempted to use them. When computing percentages, multiply the
numerator by 100 before dividing to avoid loss of low-order digits.

* Do not use redefines when a structure name will accomplish the same
thing, which is true most of the time. For example:

05  number-x.                              *> good
    10  number-9           pic  9(04).

05  number-x               pic  x(04).     *> bad
05  number-9 redefines number-x  pic  9(04).

Chapter 5

Logic

When you were in the sixth grade, you were challenged by math problems
along the line 'Mary has twice as many apples as John, who has three
apples more than Peter ...'. Beyond a low level of complexity, you had
trouble visualizing and solving these problems. In later school years
you were taught algebra. What a tool! Using its rules enabled you to
mechanically and reliably perform operations on expressions of any
complexity. You were no longer limited by your skills of intuition and
English language experience.

There is algebra for manipulating logical expressions. Because they
don't know it, most programmers are trying to solve logical algebra
problems with sixth-grade skills. They try to deal with logic, the
bread and butter of programming, armed only with inadequate common
sense and English language skills. They try to simplify complex
conditional statements by disbursing them. When their program really
needs to test whether (a > b and c = 0) or e or f, they'll make the
first part of the test in one statement and the second part in
another, cutting and pasting the same actions under each, or
performing the same paragraph. In the worst case, they'll copy the
program, give it another name, and instruct the human operator to run
the new program when e or f are true. That doesn't simplify the
program, that makes it more complex. When a program really needs to
test a complex conditional, it will accomplish it *one way or another*.
One right way is to write the conditional succinctly in a single
expression; another is to attempt to hide it in a misdirected quest
for simplicity.

Knowing how to transform a conditional of any complexity into its
negative is a powerful skill. Suppose you start with

if  (a > b and c = 0) or e or f

The negative could be written as

if  not ((a > b and c = 0) or e or f))    *> hard for most to understand

if  (a > b and c = 0) or e or f           *> convoluted, stepping over itself
        continue
else
        ....

If      (a not > b or c not = 0) and not e and not  f   *> the right way

The algebraic rules for performing this transformation are:

.. Leave parentheses unchanged
.. Change AND to OR, OR to AND
.. Change = to not = and vice versa
.. Change > to not > and vice versa
.. Change < to not < and vice versa
.. Change condition to not condition and vice versa

Mastering this skill will give you confidence that you can
mechanically manipulate conditionals of any complexity using algebra
rather than intuition.

For example, reversing this with common sense is almost impossible:

if  (a > b and c not = d and e and f) or
    g or
    (h not < zero and (h = 1 or 2))

Using the algebra you just learned, you can easily transform it to:

If  (a not > b or c = d or not e or not f) and
    not g and
    (h < zero or (h not = 1 and 2))

At the lowest level, logical variables that are false have a value of
zero, those that are true have a value of 1 (or any non-zero). AND
means multiplication whereas OR means addition. Thus 'a OR b' means
add the two. If either is non-zero, the result will be non-zero. 'a
AND b' means multiply the two. If either is zero, the result will be
zero. Understanding this explains why the precedence rules for logic
-- AND comes first before OR -- are consistent with the precedence
rules for arithmetic, where multiplication comes before addition. Some
compilers actually use machine language arithmetic rather than logic
instructions to evaluate conditionals. .

Cobol is the only programming language that lets you elide (leave out)
the subject and relational operator. This is less verbose than other
languages.

if  a =  1 or 2 or 3
Rather than
if  a = 1 or a = 2 or a = 3

Cobol gives the choice of writing "=" or "equal" or "equal to"; ">" or
"greater" or "greater than". I prefer words over logographs (symbols)
because they are consistent with the rest of the program, which is
written in English words. I'll use symbols when doing so makes the
difference between one line and two. Replacing "=" with "equal to"
doesn't make the program more verbose. Both represent a single idea.

Chapter 6

Call

Call is a very powerful function because it provides access to large
libraries, including those supplied with and written for other
languages. For example, the C++ library and GUI libraries. Other
languages get their power from libraries, not from the language
itself. If you can call their libraries, you can do anything they can
.. with more readable code.

Call is also powerful because it lets you design non-trivial systems
into source modules of manageable size rather than writing large
monolithic programs. The Demo below is a good example. As you gain
experience, you'll learn to put reusable code into callable modules
rather than cutting and pasting it into multiple programs. By
eliminating redundant code, you simplify maintenance, simplify testing
(unit test the module changed rather than the whole system) and make
the system more reliable (change code in one program but overlook
changing it elsewhere).

All calls to Cobol should be dynamically (run time) bound using dll,
so (Unix shared object) or Micro Focus gnt .. or local. Static binding
via the LINK utility is obsolete because it decreases system
reliability. You can relink the program of interest but forgot to
relink others calling the modified function. The MAKE utility was
designed to solve this problem, but programmers tend to neglect
maintaining MAKE files. Most Cobol programmers do not use MAKE files
at all. Dynamic linking solves all these problems. You recompile the
function and everyone automatically gets the newest version. Also,
there is only one copy of the function in memory and on your disk
whereas static linking proliferates redundant object code.


Here is an example of calling a local function. This is an improvement
over performing a paragraph because you can pass parameters and the
function has its own local variables (working-storage) which the
parent cannot see, and the function can see only parent variables
passed as parameters or declared global (which defeats the purpose of
writing functions). This works on all Cobol compilers because it is in
the 1985 Standard. Yet few Cobol programmers know this feature exists.

 identification division.
 program-id. demo1.
*> demo of local function
 data division.
 working-storage section.
 01  string1 value 'string1'  pic  x(30) global.
 procedure division.
 main.
     call 'function1' using string1
     goback.

 identification division.
 program-id. function1.
 data division.
 linkage section.
 01  string1a                 pic  x(30).
 procedure division using string1a.
 main.
     display string1a
     display string1    *> global variable in parent
     goback.
 end program function1.
 end program demo1.     *> note now function1 is nested within demo1

In order for this to work, the function must be nested within its
parent. This is good, because it makes you design before coding.. If you have a
utility called by multiple functions, such asmalloc in the demo below, it must
be separately compiled and dynamically linked.

In addition, Cobol permits you to write function prototypes. When
supplied, the compiler checks number and type of parameters on a call,
raising an error if they don't match. The prototype is typically
stored in a 'copybook' that is copied into the invoking program's
identification division. I did not code a prototype in the demo
because this feature is not available on all compilers. In other
languages, prototyping is so universal that it's believed to be
mandatory (it is in C++ but not in C).

Chapter 7

Data structures, linked lists

The linked list should be your first choice when the list contains a
variable number of things. Alternative choices are arrays, records in
a file and rows in a database. Entries in a linked list are
dynamically allocated and linked to each other, forward and backward,
with pointers. The size of a linked list is almost unlimited and
access is extremely fast because stepping to the next entry requires
only changing a pointer. An array, by contrast, requires a
multiplication on each subscripted reference. The Sample programs
contain many examples of using lists.

In Cobol you set the base address of a structure one time. It stays
set until you change it. Other languages require an explicit pointer
on every reference to a field, for example 'a-pointer->a-field'. Which
is more verbose? Which is more error-prone?


Chapter 8

Files and databases

There are two useful Cobol file types: line sequential (aka ASCII text
file) and indexed. Nobody uses relative and record sequential formats.
In addition, there is the 'console' - the screen and keyboard.

display 'Error ' error-code             *> write to screen
display 'Input? ' with no advancing     *> leave cursor on line
accept data-name                        *> read keyboard
accept date-today from date             *> yymmdd
accent time-now from time               *> hhmmsshh

Note that display can assemble multiple fields, similar to the string
verb. Accept does not necessarily initialize the input field; you
should clear it to spaces. Some compilers allow you to accept a number
and automatically align data on the decimal point; some do not. Some
compilers return the date as yyyymmdd if the data-name is eight bytes
long; some don't. All compilers automatically format binary numbers to
make them readable, but not pretty. Format will be 000001234+. Display
is used primarily for error messages and debugging information.
Because the screen can be directed to a file, large volume is not a
problem, however display should not be used to print a report. Reports
should be written to a plain vanilla ASCII text file. Ignore compiler
syntax about 'assign to printer' and other printer support. It is
different on every compiler and unnecessary.

 environment division.
 input-output section.
 file-control.
    select ascii-file assign to '(path file)'
        organization is line sequential
         file status is file-status.
    select indexed-file assign to '(path file)'
       organization is indexed
       access mode is dynamic
       file status is file-status
       record key is index-key.
 data division.
 file section.
 fd  ascii-file.
 01  ascii-record.
     05  ...
 fd  indexed-file.
 01  indexed-record.
     05  index-key.
         10 ...
     05  ...
 working-storage section.
 01  file-status  pic  9(02).


     open input ascii-file                 *> open sequential file
     open output ascii-file
     open i-o indexed-file                 *> open indexed file for update
     close ascii-file

     read ascii-file at end ... end-read   *> read sequential file

     move ... to index-key        *> set starting position in indexed file
     start indexed-file key not less than index-key
     read indexed-file next at end ... end-read

     write indexed-record                  *> insert a new record
     rewrite indexed-record                *> update last record read
     delete indexed-file                   *> delete last record read

If an operation fails, file-status will be non-zero. The most common
codes are 95 file not found on open, 10 end of file (not an error) and
22 insert failed because record already exists. You should test this
after every file operation.

Fujitsu uses Btrieve for its indexed file system. The other compilers
use proprietary formats. Access is amazingly fast with all.

Databases are two-dimensional Tables with rows and columns like a
spreadsheet. Operations are performed on sets of rows, not individual
records, using the SQL language. Because it is an ANSI Standard
language, SQL is exactly the same on all relational databases. If your
SQL works on Sybase, it will work on Oracle and Informix with no
changes. Embedded SQL in a Cobol program looks like:

exec sql
    select name, phone from database-name where city = 'New York';
end exec

This code is not compiled nor executed on your machine. At compile
time the select statement is assigned a handle and stored in the
database description file, called a Schema. The select statement is
replaced with a call to the database engine passing as parameters the
handle and any working-storage fields referenced. When the call is
executed, the select statement is compiled and executed by a database
server.

When the select statement is executed, a temporary table containing
the 8,000,000 residents of New York is constructed in server memory.
To access them, a Cobol program uses a device called a Cursor (no
relation to the cursor on your screen) and the SQL Fetch verb to read
rows one at a time, like reading records from a file. If the program
doesn't need to see the rows one at a time, just wants to perform some
operation on all of them, it can do so with a single SQL command.

SQL statements can contain complex logic because one statement can
Join multiple tables and they can be nested. For example:

select name, phone from phone-table where customer in (select customer
from customer-table where city in (select city from salesman-table
where status = 'active'));

A way to simplify SQL is by using Temp Tables, which permit you to do
one operation at a time and look at the intermediate results with an
appropriate query tool. The best query tool is Msquery, which comes
with Excel. It imports the result of a SQL query into a spreadsheet,
where you can perform operations on it, then write the data back into
the database table(s) or temp table.

Informix makes it easy to create Temp Tables whereas Oracle pretends
they don't exist (they do, you just have to work harder to create
them). All databases except Oracle use the 'SQL 3' ANSI Standard dated
1998; Oracle uses the 'SQL 2' Standard dated 1989. The differences are
mainly in the area of administration rather than application
programming. Oracle assumes the Database Administrator (DBA) is logged
on to the database server and uses proprietary programs for
administration. Other databases permit remote administration via SQL
sent through ODBC. Generally, the best databases are Informix and SQL
Server, although Oracle has a superior query optimizer. Optimizers can
exploit parallel processing by launching multiple tasks running on
multiple processors, each doing a 'subquery'. Optimizers are also
intelligent, as illustrated in the next paragraph. Optimizers can
dramatically improve speed of a query.

You can roll your own database-like optimizations on indexed Cobol
files. Suppose you want to find all customers of salesman 1234 living
in Dallas. The query can be done two ways: by reading all customers in
Dallas looking for that salesman, or by reading all customers of the
salesman looking for city equals Dallas. You want to quickly determine
the number of customers belonging to that salesman and the number of
customers in Dallas. The smaller of the two numbers will be the
fastest query. To do that, you start/read next salesman 1234 in a file
keyed by salesman, get the record number and remember it. Then
start/read next salesman 1235, get the number of his first record. The
difference is the number of records coded to 1234. In a file keyed by
city, do the same for 'Dallas' and 'Dallaszzzz'. The method of getting
the relative record number is different for each compiler. This is how
it is done with Micro Focus:

$SET FCDREG
 linkage section.
 01  fcd.
     copy '$COBDIR/cpylib/XFHFCD.CPY'.

     move '1234' to primary-key of salesman-file
     start salesman-file key not less than primary-key of salesman-file
     read salesman-file next at end .... end-read
     set address of fcd to address of fh--fcd of salesman-file
     move fcd-relative-key to beginning-record-number

Chapter 9

Lookup

01  array-values.
    05  filler value '0 Sunday'             pic  x(10).     *> value
    05  filler value '1 Monday'             pic  x(10).
         ...
    05  filler value '6 Saturday'           pic  x(10).
01  searchable-array redefines array-values.                *> redefines
    05  array-element indexed x-array ascending-key antecedent occurs 7.
        10  antecedent                      pic  9(01).*> key for SEARCH ALL
        10  filler                          pic  x(01).
        10  consequent                      pic  x(08).

Lookup is done with the Search verb. There are two types:

Serial (sequantial):

set x-array to 1
search array-element
    at end (do something)
    when antecedent (x-array) = da-of-week [continue or do something]
end-search

Array must be described with an index, which you must initialize to 1.
Speed is proportional to n/2.

Binary:

search all array-element
    at end (do something)
    when antecedent (x-array) = da-of-week [continue or do something]
end-search

Array must be described with an index, which you need not initialize,
and a key clause. Array must be sorted in key order. When clause may
only compare whether key is equal to something. Speed is proportional
to ln(n), making it much faster than serial.

When the number of table elements is variable, use 'occurs ..
depending on ' to specify the size at execution time. Do not pad it
with high-values and waste time searching through them.

Note that search generates in-line code, not a call to a library
function nor even necessarily a loop. This can make a big speed
difference when searching an array of bytes. All languages can find a
byte in a string, but only Cobol can do it with a single machine
instruction: 'repne scasb'. While testing the Sieve, a standard
benchmark which spends most of its time searching, Realia Cobol code
ran faster than every other compiler.

Chapter 10

Sorting

There are two types of sort:

Sorting an array:

sort array-element on ascending antecedent
sort array-element              *> uses key in array description

If the array has a key clause, it will use that by default.
Array-based sorting is now available only on Micro Focus. It will soon
be available on all compilers because it is in the 2002 Standard.

Sorting a file:

 environment division.
 input-output section.
 file-control.
    select input-file ...
    select sortwork assign to 'sortwork'.       *> sortwork file
    select output-file ...

 data division.
 file section.
 fd  input-file.
 ...
 sd  sortwork.                                  *> sd rather than fd
 01  sort-record.
     05  sort-key.
         10  ...
         10  ...
     05  sort-body.
         10  ...
 fd  output-file.
 ...

 procedure division.
 sort-driver.
     sort sortwork on ascending sort-key        *> sort statement
         input procedure input-procedure
         output procedure output-procedure
     goback.

 input-procedure.
     open input input-file
     perform read-input
     perform until end-of-input
         (compose sort record)
         release sort-record                    *> release verb
         perform read-input
     end-perform
     close input-file.
 output-procedure.
     open output output-file
     perform read-sort
     perform until end-of-sort
         (compose output record)
         write output-record
         perform read-sort
     end-perform
     close output-file.
 read-sort.
     return sortwork at end                     *> return verb
         set end-of-sort to true
     end-return.

Think of this as three job steps: a program that builds a temp file, a
sort on that file, a program which uses that file. The most common use
is printing reports.

roll your own

If you want to write your own sort, the fastest algorithm is not
quicksort, as commonly thought, but rather radix sort on the first few
positions followed by insertion sort. See Sedgwick's classic book on
algorithms, not Knuth's. I have measured this hybrid sort running 21
times the speed of quicksort. Sorting lists is much faster than
sorting arrays because exchanging entries involves a few pointer swaps
rather than moving the whole record around. Interestingly, radix was
the algorithm used by card sorters in the 50's; insertion was the
algorithm used by card collators.

Do not roll your own bubble sort. It is at least 50 times slower than
any other algorithm. Do not insert records into an indexed file or
database just to sort them.


Chapter 11

Graphic User Interface (GUI)

Cobol does not natively support GUI. Neither does C, C++ or Basic. All
rely on an outside tool to construct and present the GUI screen,
passing data to your program for processing. The best tool for Cobol
is sp2 by Flexus. A free 60-day evaluation can be downloaded from
http://www.flexus.com/download.html. The normal retail price is $1,200
but for single-user, non-commercial developers it's $300. Your screens
will be indistinguishable from VB and C++.

In theory, a Cobol program could call MFC (Microsoft Foundation
Classes), first generation GUI, or OLE/COM (Component Object Model),
second generation GUI, or dot-NET, third generation GUI, all of which
are free from Microsoft. The problem is understanding the format of
objects passed back and forth. You could spend hundreds of hours
figuring this out.

Designing a screen is as easy with sp2 as with VB and VC++. You drag
and drop widgets, then give them names your program will use to
address the data. Sp2 writes your program's framework. The program
doesn't have numerous 'callbacks', it has a single entry and exit
point.

I have worked with many screen managers, including Fujitsu's Power
Cobol and Micro Focus' Net Express. Sp2 is the best I've seen.
```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net...

I'm going to start with a suggestion that I originally had at the end:

Get copies of ANSI X3.23-1985 and ISO/IEC 1989:2002 and find out what's
actually in them.  I'm afraid you've consistently confused "conventional in
the implementations with which I'm familiar" with "standard".  If you're
indeed writing a book on Best Practices, I think you'd agree that the
investment in these two documents would reasonably be considered appropriate
to ensuring a quality product.  Go through everything you've written, and
check to see whether it's described in, and consistent with, the appropriate
standard.  This is particularly important for those things you describe as
standard in the first place.

Herewith some further comments:

> Basics, divisions and sections
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> display 'hello world'.

Standard '85 COBOL doesn't require ENVIRONMENT or DATA DIVISIONs, but does
require IDENTIFICATION and PROCEDURE.  And standard '02 COBOL requires
PROGRAM-ID even if it does allow the omission of the associated division
header.  PROGRAM-ID gets REALLY powerful in '02 COBOL.

> The data division has three sections-file, working-storage and
> linkage-in which you describe data structures. All three follow the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> passed by a calling program or dynamically allocated.  ...

Ignoring, of course, the fact that you describe the meaty details of FILEs
in the data division, and the fact that it also contains the REPORT and
COMMUNICATION SECTIONs of '85 COBOL, and the LOCAL-STORAGE and SCREEN
SECTIONs of '02 COBOL.  I know you're only talking about the ones you're
interested in, but there are more.

> Historically, Cobol was stored on punched cards and had rules saying
> columns 1-6 contained a sequence number, so you could sort the cards
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> rules went away eighteen years ago, in 1985,

Nope.  Still in '85 standard COBOL, still the default in '02 standard COBOL.
See prior message.  Overridable only in standards-compliant compilers issued
within the last ten weeks or so, or as a vendor-specific extension to
earlier ones.  The Column-72 location of Margin R did go away in '85, but
not the column locations of Margins L, C, A or B.    And Margin B (but not
Margins L, C or A, or the existence of Margin R) went away altogether in
fixed-form reference format in '02 standard COBOL.

>Position 1 is now for the asterisk formerly in column 7, line position 2
through a very big
> limit contains your program text.

Not  unless 1) ">>SOURCE FORMAT FREE" is in effect, 2) the compiler includes
both the Free-Form and Compiler Directive features of '02 COBOL, AND 3) the
"*" is followed immediately by ">".

>You can write comments to the right
> of program text by using *>.

True in '02 COBOL, but in '85 COBOL only with implementor-defined
extensions.

>Best Practice: use lower case and "free
> form", but avoid creating lines (much) longer than your text editor
> can display without scrolling.

Lower case is strictly a style issue from '85 COBOL onward; since then,
COBOL as a language is case-independent.  Free format source is only in '02
COBOL or earlier compilers that include it as an implementor-defined
extension (that fact should be getting pretty clear by now).

>
> Chapter 2
>
> Basics, data types and structures
>
 Strings:
>
> 01  a-string                           pic  x(30).     *> string
> 01  b-string                           pic  x(70).     *> string
>
> A Cobol string is fixed length, filled with spaces on the right.

Only sometimes.  STRING doesn't fill, as you point out later.  Whether and
how it's filled or not is not dependent on the USAGE, it's dependent on the
VERB.

> Convert Cobol to C
> string cobol-string delimited by '  '  low-value into c-string
> *> look for 2 spaces

Not in standard COBOL.  Use delimited by <2-character-data-name>.  This is
discussed further later on.

> .. and Pascal-style strings with an explicit length.
>     05  pascal-structure.     *> pascal-style string
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>      05  cobol-string               pic  x(30).

Are you sure *ALL* Pascal implementations comply with this?

> The rules for moving structure names are the same as
> strings-truncation when smaller, space-fill when larger.

Except when they're not ... See the rules for MOVE and the specific
exception involving group MOVEs.

> Numbers:
> 01  an-integer           comp-5  pic s9(09).     *> four bytes, 32 bits
> 01  half-word            comp-5  pic s9(04).     *> two bytes, 16 bits
> 01  quarter-word         comp-5  pic s9(02).     *> one byte, 8 bits
> 01  long-integer         comp-5  pic s9(15).     *> eight bytes, 64bits

Neither the '85 nor the '02 standard defines the internal format of COMP,
and COMP-5 is not in any of the standards at all.  These formats are always
defined by the implementor of the language for the particular intended
platform.

> These are integers stored in the machine's natural, highest-speed,
> format.

On some machines in some dialects of COBOL, maybe, but COMP-5 isn't
standard.  BINARY works pretty well on the machines I'm familiar with, and
it IS standard syntax.    But again the format is implementor defined.

> Although a 16-bit word can hold values to 32,767, your Cobol compiler
> may not allow values greater than 9,999 because pic 9(04) means four
> decimal digits.

It better, at least for DISPLAY and COMP.  If you add 1 to a PIC 9(4)
DISPLAY item containing 9999, you'd better end up with 0.  The rules about
the "odometer effect" are very, very basic to COBOL, and they'd better be
applied unless you're off in a vendor extension somewhere (like PIC 9 BINARY
EXTENDED, which allows values as high as 549,755,813,887 on any machine
worth its salt ... )

> All numbers stored in files should be this type. The number is in
> ASCII characters.

Unless it's not -- EBCDIC, for example, or a national character set, or ...

>  ... The sign is over the
> low-order digit as 'A' for +1 and 'J' for -1.

Unless it's not -- (all) the standards all allow the implementor to define
the default position and representation of the sign and allow the user to
override it with the SIGN clause for individual data declarations.

> The size limit of numbers is 15 digits plus a sign.

Nope.  '68, '74 and '85 COBOL have required implementors to allow at least
18 digits.  The '68 standard required support for at least 18 digits but
allowed more; the '74 standard made it clear that 18 digits were required
and that anything more was an extension.

>
> 01  packed-decimal-number      comp-3  pic s9(08)v9(07).
>
> Packed is an obsolete format where each digit and the sign occupy half
> a byte, called a nybble (cute, huh?).

Obsolete?  "PACKED DECIMAL" was introduced as a standard USAGE in '85 COBOL.
On some machines plain old COMP is defined as packed decimal.  On some
machines it's VERY efficient indeed.

> Historically, operations on
> packed numbers were faster than on display because machines offered
> hardware support. Modern machines do not, so there is no reason to use
> packed numbers.

Hardware support for things like decimal conversion and decimal arithmetic
(and for that matter, things like editing) is not necessarily dead!

> Indicators (flags):
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> false value.

What about USAGE BIT ('02 standard)?   Seems like it might have some sort of
use in "true-false" situations ...

> Pointers:

'02 COBOL only.  Not in '85 standard COBOL, and no guarantee that
implementations that support this syntax are compatible with each other.

> Pointers contain memory addresses, which are 32-bit.

Unless they're not.  The format of pointers is explicitly left to the
implementor by the '02 standard.

> Arrays:

The term used in the standard is "Tables".

> See pascal-structure above for a variable length array. Indexes, such
> as x-arrray, are useful only for searching. Historically, they were
> provided as a high-speed alternative to subscripts. Nowadays, linked
> lists, used extensively in the Demo, are faster and more flexible.

I doubt that.  Can you prove it mathematically in the general case?

> Floating-point:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Format is ANSI-standard, compatible with other programming languages.

Not in '85 standard COBOL, and not using that syntax in '02 COBOL, either.
FLOAT-SHORT, FLOAT-LONG, FLOAT-EXTENDED, maybe.  But even there the FORMAT
of these floating-point variables is ... wait for it ...
implementor-defined.

> move low-values to group-name            *> binary zeros, strings only

Most likely result, but not defined that way in the standard, and could be
different depending on the way the implementor handles character sets.

> move high-values to a                    *> FF, strings only

Machines and implementations that use an 8-bit character, maybe, but what
about 6-bit?  Internationalized programs?

>move x'0C' to a                          *> hex literal, strings only, will
be

Extension in '85 COBOL, uses different syntax in '02.

> compute a = (b * c) / d         *> receiving field must be number or
edited
> compute p rounded = (n * 100) / d
> compute b = a                   *> same as move a to b

... but COMPUTE has not only ROUNDED but ON SIZE ERROR, which MOVE lacks.


> add 1 to n
> subtract b from c
> multiply b by c
> divide b into c
> divide 7 into century-date giving week-number remainder da-of-week

> Note that the receiving field is right-most word in all cases.

No, not in the last case.  TO, GIVING, INTO.  It is true that MULTIPLY A BY
B puts the result in B, which is why it's probably better to use GIVING.  If
the compiler's not smart enough to treat MULTIPLY A BY B GIVING B the same
as MULTIPLY A BY B, then you need to yell at your vendor.

>Cobol offers other syntax that reverses word order. Use these forms only.

Why?  I'd say it's more useful to use the forms that are CLEAR TO YOU AS A
PROGRAMMER.   Particularly for MULTIPLY I don't think the CORRECT behavior
is OBVIOUS.

> Generally, compute is preferred over elemental arithmetic with the
> exception of 'add 1', simple addition to an accumulator and cases
> where you need the remainder. Compare Cobol's 'add a-number to total'
> to other languages' 'total = total + a-number'. Which is more verbose?

Which is clearer?  I tend to prefer COMPUTE only in cases where I have an
arithmetic expression that involves more than one arithmetic operation.

> Evaluate is Cobol's 'case statement'. It is used in place of if when
> there is more than one condition. Using it avoids inappropriate
> nesting of ifs. ...

Evaluate is actually much more powerful than e.g., Pascal or ALGOL's CASE.

> There are two types of perform: in-line loop control and a 'call' to
> another paragraph.

I think lumping "PERFORM" with "CALL" is misleading.

>In the old days, calling another paragraph was the only form.

Unless you wanted to call another program (introduced in '74 COBOL).  How
old is old?

In-line PERFORM was introduced in the '85 standard.

>Nowadays, loop control is the predominant use. Using
> perform to 'call' a paragraph is weak, like Basic's GOSUB, because it
> doesn't permit you to pass parameters. It is preferable to use the
> call verb to call local functions.

Arguable.  Depends entirely on the implementation.

>> call 'program' using a, b, c

> Passing control and parameters to another program, statically or
> dynamically linked, written in Cobol or another language. Can also be
> used to call a local function, similar to perform with the addition of
> parameters. Chapter 6 and the Demo show how to do this.

Some machines don't do "static linking" for this.  Neither '74, '85 nor '02
COBOL standards specify how programs in a run unit are linked together.

>> move spaces to a-string
> string 'Error ' error-code delimited by size into a-string *> compose
'Error xx'
> string city delimited by '  ' ', ' state space zip delimited by size
> into a-string
> *> the above delimiter is two spaces
> string cobol-string delimited by '  ', low-value into c-string

The second STRING will step on the information put into a-string.  Don't you
want a WITH POINTER clause here?

DELIMITED BY syntax allows a single delimiter; DELIMITED BY "  " (where the
literal is two spaces) is OK, but "DELIMITED BY " ", " " (two one-space
literals) is not.  Neither is "DELIMITED BY " ", LOW-VALUE.  Try DELIMITED
BY a two-character data item containing the desired value.  Or, if your
implementation allows it (it's not standard), DELIMITED BY x"2000", or
whatever syntax it uses for hex literals.

 > move spaces to input-words
> unstring input-line delimited by all space into
>     input-word (1) input-word (2) input-word (3) ...
> *> all space means multiple spaces are treated as one delimiter

Nope.  Standard disallows "ALL" before any sort of literal -- figurative or
otherwise -- in this context.  ALL <dataname>, yes; ALL <literal>, no, ALL
SPACE, no.

> Neither string nor unstring fills its output with spaces; you must
> initialize.

So MOVE is better than STRING or UNSTRING?  I think that's a bit of a broad
brush!

And do you mean the INITIALIZE verb?  I'd expect it'd most likely step on
the results, unless you mean *pre*-initialize.  Why not UNSTRING ... WITH
POINTER followed by a STRING SPACES WITH POINTER?

> Unstring lacks power. For instance, it cannot preserve quoted
> sub-strings. For non-trivial use, you'll wind up writing your own
> parser, as I did in the Demo Program. Unstring is useful for
> converting a null-terminated C-style string to a space-filled
> Cobol-style string.

Well, I just don't know what to do with that.

>inspect a-string converting
>     'ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ' to
>     'abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz'

> Inspect is an overly complex verb which isn't particularly useful.
> You can program its functions yourself with more confidence they will
> work as desired.

I've used it often to good, and more importantly, contextually clear effect.

> It has one useful function: translating one character
> set to another.

If you're talking about your example of INSPECT ... CONVERTING, only in '02
COBOL and implementations that have extended their '85 compilers beyond the
applicable standard to include it.

>On older compilers, this function was the Transform verb.

Never heard of TRANSFORM.  Which standard was that in?  I can't find it in
'68, '74, '85 or '02.   Did find '68 COBOL's EXAMINE being replaced by the
more powerful INSPECT in '74 COBOL though.

 > goback
>
> This is how you exit, returning to the calling program or operating
> system. Goback replaces 'stop run' and 'exit program'.

Not in *standard* '85 COBOL, though many (by no means all!) implementations
provide it.

> Chapter 4

...

Well, I think I've put about as much effort into this as I can afford right
now.  See my suggestion at the beginning.

     -Chuck Stevens
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T16:16:21+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b21srq$bt0$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
Chuck Stevens wrote:

>> add 1 to n
>> subtract b from c
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, not in the last case.  TO, GIVING, INTO.  

Forget the REMAINDER clause ..

> It is true that MULTIPLY A BY
> B puts the result in B, which is why it's probably better to use GIVING. 

Originally Cobol was designed to use the final word as the destination as 
this made it easier to remember:

     MOVE N         TO R
     ADD N          TO R
     SUBTRACT N   FROM R
     MULTIPLY N     BY R
     DIVIDE N     INTO R

adding a GIVING retained this convention, the result was still the last 
word:

     MULTIPLY N      BY M    GIVING R

It was was almost compromised when users wanted a DIVIDE BY, and this is 
why this must always be

     DIVIDE N   BY M  GIVING R

You cannot have DIVIDE BY without a GIVING, did you not know why this was ?

> If the compiler's not smart enough to treat MULTIPLY A BY B GIVING B the
> same as MULTIPLY A BY B, then you need to yell at your vendor.

There is more to the design of the language than just whether they 
accidentally choose the 'right' way to do it.

>>Cobol offers other syntax that reverses word order. Use these forms only.
> 
> Why?  I'd say it's more useful to use the forms that are CLEAR TO YOU AS A
> PROGRAMMER.   Particularly for MULTIPLY I don't think the CORRECT behavior
> is OBVIOUS.

Once you understand the uniform rule for the original Cobol design it gets 
easier.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T06:07:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e449e82.203517369@news.optonline.net>`

```

>Once you understand the uniform rule for the original Cobol design it gets 
>easier.

The universal rule involves word order, which for Cobol is verb-subject-object
(VSO). 


People who design programming languages should have at a least a litttle
background in linquistics. Computer Science geeks who design languages have
heard of Noam Chomsky, but not read any of his books, especially his seminal
Syntactic Structures. As a result, we're stuck with 'techie' languages which
just don't get it.
```

', ' is a literal to output:

    string city      delimited by '  ' 
           ', ' 
           state 
           space 
           zip       delimited by size
        into a-string

will result in: "NEW YORK, NY 12345" for example.

     string cobol-string delimited by '  '
            low-value 
         into c-string

will result in a nul (low-value) terminated value in c-string.

Given that 'initialize' implies 'at the start' then why would you think 
that he would be 'initializing' afterwards (as in "step on results") ?

> POINTER followed by a STRING SPACES WITH POINTER?

I don't that would be particularly useful way of doing it, the UNSTING 
POINTER is the position in the source string, you seem to want to use it on 
the destination.

Did you mean ? :
             UNSTRING ... 
                INTO this COUNT IN this-size
             STRING SPACES 
                 INTO this WITH POINTER this-size

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T08:55:01-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b28lh6$17se$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <b21ufk$cl3$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`

```

"Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:b21ufk$cl3$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz...
> No.  You misread the statement, there is only one delimiter specified, the
> ', ' is a literal to output:

Right.  I did misunderstand the intent.

> > Nope.  Standard disallows "ALL" before any sort of literal -- figurative
> > or
> > otherwise -- in this context.  ALL <dataname>, yes; ALL <literal>, no,
ALL
> > SPACE, no.
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>           INTO ..

I don't know what your references are.  My reference on this particular
subject is ANSI X3.23-1985 Page VI-136, 6.27, "THE UNSTRING STATEMENT",
Syntax Rule 1, which states:  "Literal-1 and literal-2 must be nonnumeric
literals and neither can be a figurative constant that begins with ALL."   I
can't see how that can be read as allowing UNSTRING ... DELIMITED BY ALL
SPACES ... as standard COBOL.  When a figurative is used (without ALL,
obviously) in this context, it stands for a single character (General Rule
8), but with ALL, how many characters would be considered to satisfy the
criterion?   If two is uncondionally sufficient, then an alphanumeric
literal "  " works fine.  If what you're really looking for is "delimited by
trailing spaces", then the REVERSE function provides this capability in
standards-conformant COBOL (the intrinsics function amendment is mandatory
for conformance to '85 COBOL, and of course the functionality remains in
2002 COBOL).

> > And do you mean the INITIALIZE verb?  I'd expect it'd most likely step
on
> > the results, unless you mean *pre*-initialize.
>
> Given that 'initialize' implies 'at the start' then why would you think
> that he would be 'initializing' afterwards (as in "step on results") ?

"INITIALIZE" is a verb that may be used anywhere in the program, and he was
indicating the necessity to pre-initialize the destination (say, to spaces)
before the two verbs within the program.

>
> > Why not UNSTRING ... WITH
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> POINTER is the position in the source string, you seem to want to use it
on
> the destination.
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes, probably; point being, it's doable.  Some vendors also have extensions
that make this some easier:  "FOR <numeric-size>" in both verbs, for
example.  But I agree they're not standard COBOL.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

```
Chuck Stevens wrote:

>> Not according to my references:
>>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> 2002 COBOL).

An optional ALL is part of the syntax.  A literal (as in literal-1) may be 
"ALL SPACES" for example.  You may not use ALL in the literal to get:

       DELIMITED BY ALL ALL SPACES

where the first ALL is part of the syntax and the 2nd ALL is part of the 
literal.  Hence they ban literals of the form 'figurative constant that 
begins with ALL'.

So DELIMITED BY ALL SPACE or DELIMITED BY ALL ", " or DELIMITED BY ALL 
Separator are available in the standard.

With this ALL any numbe of adjacent delimiters are traeated as one.
 
> "INITIALIZE" is a verb that may be used anywhere in the program, 

But it is also an english word that has a root that means 'at the start'.
```

My understanding is that both SPACE and ALL SPACE are

ALL, those figurative constants that begin with ALL are
prohibited. Essentially, the rule is stating that ALL ALL SPACE
may not occur in an UNSTRING statement; but ALL SPACE may.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T11:56:35-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b2905k$1fcm$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <b21ufk$cl3$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <b28lh6$17se$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
My apologies to the readership.

In response to Richard Plinston, I wrote:

> I don't know what your references are.  My reference on this particular
> subject is ANSI X3.23-1985 Page VI-136, 6.27, "THE UNSTRING STATEMENT",
> Syntax Rule 1, which states:  "Literal-1 and literal-2 must be nonnumeric
> literals and neither can be a figurative constant that begins with ALL."
...

Bill Klein pointed out to me (very kindly and diplomatically, I might add!)
that what is actually nonconforming is "UNSTRING ... ALL ALL ...

The first "ALL" is part of the UNSTRING syntax, not part of the literal.
And that, for what it's worth, means that in the fragment
    UNSTRING ... DELIMITED BY ALL "Z"
'ALL "Z" ' is NOT treated as a figurative constant.

UNSTRING ... DELIMITED BY ALL SPACES is indeed allowed by the standards.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T18:32:19+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
"Russell Styles" <RWSTYLES@comcast.net> wrote:

>
>major snip
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>using each byte as an index into a 256 byte lookup table.

Correct. Intel has the same instruction.

IBM also had Translate and Test (TRT), whose major use was not translation at
all but rather scanning for a delimiter. Intel has a similar instruction in
'repne scasb', which at least one COBOL compiler -- Realia -- uses when you
SEARCH an array of bytes looking for equal to a single value. It's very fast.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T04:32:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01c2cff4$34236560$b491f243@chottel>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <daadnWOAhPGeXdmjXTWcog@comcast.com>`

```
IBM's COBOL had TRANSFORM back in 1972 when I started.  The assembler
language instruction is TR or TRANSLATE not TRANSFORM.

Russell Styles <RWSTYLES@comcast.net> wrote in article
<daadnWOAhPGeXdmjXTWcog@comcast.com>...
> 
> major snip
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > Never heard of TRANSFORM.  Which standard was that in?  I can't find it
in
> > '68, '74, '85 or '02.   Did find '68 COBOL's EXAMINE being replaced by
the
> > more powerful INSPECT in '74 COBOL though.
> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> an interesting assembler verb.  In IBM assembler (at least back in
360/370
> days)
> Transform was an assembler verb.  Not macro - just assembler.  Amounts to

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Steve Thompson <s_thompson#NO#SPAM_@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T23:56:01-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3E45DF61.ED670FC@ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <daadnWOAhPGeXdmjXTWcog@comcast.com>`

```
Russell Styles wrote:
<snip> 
>     I suspect that transform was a case of IBM feeling obliged to use
> an interesting assembler verb.  In IBM assembler (at least back in 360/370
> days)
> Transform was an assembler verb.  Not macro - just assembler.  Amounts to
> using each byte as an index into a 256 byte lookup table.

Well, I don't exactly know where you get this. The "VERB" in
BAL or ALC was TR for TRanslate or TRT for TRanslate & Test.
In both cases, this is a MNEMONIC representing a specific
OPCODE.

TR was used to translate from ASCII to EBCDIC or vice versa
(as just ONE example).

TRT was used to locate a particular character(s) (say you
wanted to parse/scan some text and wanted to find all the
delimiters) by making that particular offset into the table
contain other than x'00'.
```
