##### ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T18:22:33+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>standard in the first place.

I would if the standards were available electronically. I'm a mobile contract
programmer who cannot transport a library of paper every time I move, about once
a year. Why do the cobbler's children have no shoes? 

When I'm not certain a feature is in the Standard, I'll change references from
"the standard" to "standard practice". Free-form is a good example. I've been
using it since 1984.

>> The data division has three sections-file, working-storage and
>> linkage-in which you describe data structures. All three follow the
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>in the data division...

They have evaporated over the years. Nowadays, it's fd file-name (period). 

>and the fact that it also contains the REPORT and
>COMMUNICATION SECTIONs of '85 COBOL, and the LOCAL-STORAGE and SCREEN
>SECTIONs of '02 COBOL.  I know you're only talking about the ones you're
>interested in, but there are more.

Searching tens of thousands of programs found no use of those at Merrill Lynch,
Sears (both mainframe), US Dept of Education (Unix) and other IT shops large and
small. As I said in the introduction, this tutorial pares COBOL down to
essentials by omitting features not used in practice. 

>Lower case is strictly a style issue from '85 COBOL onward; since then,
>COBOL as a language is case-independent.  Free format source is only in '02
>COBOL or earlier compilers that include it as an implementor-defined
>extension (that fact should be getting pretty clear by now).

We users desire standards to minimize implementor-defined features. We generally
avoid features that are different between compilers.

>> A Cobol string is fixed length, filled with spaces on the right.
>
>Only sometimes.  STRING doesn't fill, as you point out later.  Whether and
>how it's filled or not is not dependent on the USAGE, it's dependent on the
>VERB.

The statement is valid in a tutorial introducing neophytes to COBOL. 

>> Convert Cobol to C
>> string cobol-string delimited by '  '  low-value into c-string
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>discussed further later on.

In the discussion below, you say '  ' (two spaces) is valid.

>> .. and Pascal-style strings with an explicit length.
>>     05  pascal-structure.     *> pascal-style string
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>Are you sure *ALL* Pascal implementations comply with this?

No. I'll change "Pascal" to "variable-length strings".


>Neither the '85 nor the '02 standard defines the internal format of COMP,
>and COMP-5 is not in any of the standards at all.  These formats are always
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>it IS standard syntax.    But again the format is implementor defined.

COMP-5 means 'native' on all mainstream compilers. As you point out, COMP and
BINARY are interpreted differently by various compilers. De-facto standards are
useful knowledge for the practitioner. 

>> All numbers stored in files should be this type. The number is in
>> ASCII characters.
>
>Unless it's not -- EBCDIC, for example, or a national character set, or ...

Whoops. Change "ASCII" to "displayable".

>>  ... The sign is over the
>> low-order digit as 'A' for +1 and 'J' for -1.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>override it with the SIGN clause for individual data declarations.

Sign over low-order digit is another de-facto standard.

>> The size limit of numbers is 15 digits plus a sign.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>and that anything more was an extension.

You are correct. Thanks for catching the error. 

The limit of 15 was for packed on a mainframe, because machine language
instructions provided four bits for the length plus sign.

>> 01  packed-decimal-number      comp-3  pic s9(08)v9(07).
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>machines it's VERY efficient indeed.

I'll change the text to say packed is efficient on mainframes and possibly other
machines designed years ago. 

>What about USAGE BIT ('02 standard)?   Seems like it might have some sort of
>use in "true-false" situations ...

I'll clarify that this document describes '85 COBOL, not the '02 standard. 

>> Pointers:
>
>'02 COBOL only.  Not in '85 standard COBOL, and no guarantee that
>implementations that support this syntax are compatible with each other.

In practice, Micro Focus, IBM and Fujitsu (at least) use exactly the same syntax
in their '85 compilers. That's useful knowledge for the practitioner. 

>> Pointers contain memory addresses, which are 32-bit.
>
>Unless they're not.  The format of pointers is explicitly left to the
>implementor by the '02 standard.

Right. I'll change "are 32-bit" to "are usually 32-bit on today's machines. In
the future, the size will increase to 64-bit and beyond."

>> See pascal-structure above for a variable length array. Indexes, such
>> as x-arrray, are useful only for searching. Historically, they were
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>I doubt that.  Can you prove it mathematically in the general case?

Of course not. Speed depends on efficiency of memory managers -- both hardware
and software. But knowing that C and other languages use pointers so heavily,
it's a safe bet that machines and operating systems will be optimized to handle
pointers well. 

Allocating tables of worst-case size also rests on assumptions about memory
managers' ability to swap out the unused portions. If that doesn't happen, a
high paging rate couldl negate any apparent efficiency. 

>> Floating-point:
>>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>implementor-defined.

This is another discrepancy between imprecise ('flexible') standards and
real-world practice. The title of the paper is Best *Practices*.

>> move low-values to group-name            *> binary zeros, strings only
>
>Most likely result, but not defined that way in the standard, and could be
>different depending on the way the implementor handles character sets.

Burroughs used 'the COBOL character set', in which low-values were spaces and
high-values were EBCDIC "9". I cursed theoriticians every time I had to find a
work-around. 

>> move high-values to a                    *> FF, strings only
>
>Machines and implementations that use an 8-bit character, maybe, but what
>about 6-bit?  Internationalized programs?

6-bit? Has IBM reintroduced the 1401? 

>... but COMPUTE has not only ROUNDED but ON SIZE ERROR, which MOVE lacks.

Good point. I'll add your amplification.

>>Cobol offers other syntax that reverses word order. Use these forms only.
>
>Why?  I'd say it's more useful to use the forms that are CLEAR TO YOU AS A
>PROGRAMMER.   Particularly for MULTIPLY I don't think the CORRECT behavior
>is OBVIOUS.

The test is not what's clear to you, it's what will be clear to the NEXT
programmer who has to understand your code. A program must communicate to the
compiler, to yourself over time and to other programmers. The latter is COBOL's
strongest virtue, compared to other languages.

Consistant verb-subject-object word order improves COBOL's clarity. We say 'move
subject to object', where the receiving field is rightmost. 'multiply a by b' is
consistant with that word order. 

>> There are two types of perform: in-line loop control and a 'call' to
>> another paragraph.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>old is old?

My comment about calling another paragraph was in the context of PERFORM; it was
not in a discussion of the CALL verb. 

I think the comparison is appropriate. Every PERFORM could be replaced by a CALL
to a local function. If the function needs access to a file, one must move the
file and all operations on it into a single function, because files cannot be
passed as parameters.

>> Passing control and parameters to another program, statically or
>> dynamically linked, written in Cobol or another language. Can also be
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>COBOL standards specify how programs in a run unit are linked together.

Agreed. Most compilers do the 'linking' internally, which explains why the
function must be nested. If the compiler produced an EXTERN, the function could
be called by outside programs, thereby violating the spirit of local. 

>>> move spaces to a-string
>> string 'Error ' error-code delimited by size into a-string *> compose
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>want a WITH POINTER clause here?

They were two unrelated examples. I'll put a separator between them. 

I didn't want to convuse the reader with two meanings of the word POINTER.  In
practice, I seldom use WITH POINTER. If a string cannot be built with a single
STRING, I prefer to roll my own using subscripts. The Demo does so in
word-function, with code that could NOT be replaced by an UNSTRING verb because
it preserves quoted strings as a single word. 

>DELIMITED BY syntax allows a single delimiter; DELIMITED BY "  " (where the
>literal is two spaces) is OK, but "DELIMITED BY " ", " " (two one-space
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>whatever syntax it uses for hex literals.

The desired value is the end of the COBOL string. Granted, two spaces is an
imperfect solution because the COBOL string might contain two spaces. Moreover,
when it has a single trailing space, that space will be copied to the C string.
In my defense, line-function in the Demo shows how to scan right to left for the
last non-space character. 

> > move spaces to input-words
>> unstring input-line delimited by all space into
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>SPACE, no.

You might want to double-check. I do it all the time and it works as described.

>> Neither string nor unstring fills its output with spaces; you must
>> initialize.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>POINTER followed by a STRING SPACES WITH POINTER?

STRING SPACES, with expressed or implied DELIMITED BY SIZE, will put a single
space. One would have to write a loop to clear the receiving string one
character at a time. 

I like the idea of INITIALIZE, but don't use it because it is error-prone. It
does not clear FILLERs nor POINTERs. Hopefully, this bug was fixed in the '02
standard.

Pre-initialize is meaningless. Initialize means done at the beginning. One
cannot do something before the beginning. 

>>[INSPECT] has one useful function: translating one character
>> set to another.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>applicable standard to include it.

In practice, all major '85 compilers have that feature, and syntax is identical.

>>On older compilers, this function was the Transform verb.
>
>Never heard of TRANSFORM.  Which standard was that in?  I can't find it in
>'68, '74, '85 or '02.  

It was an IBM mainframe COBOL invention which has been available since the days
of '68 COBOL. Micro Focus and Realia copied it.

>Well, I think I've put about as much effort into this as I can afford right
>now.  See my suggestion at the beginning.
>
>     -Chuck Stevens

Thank you for the lengthy critique. I appreciate the time you spent.

Robert
```

```
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>    End-perform.

You may want to make this DELIMITED BY ALL SPACE to reduce the number if 
blank words that you extract between the actual words.  It may also make it 
faster as it will skip all the trailing spaces at the end of MyText rather 
than all the work required for several hundred MyWord()s, it would be 
faster to space fill MyWords before this code.  Of course you should also 
change the UNTIL to "MyPointer > MyText-Length OR MyIndex > 500", but you 
should have this anyway.
```

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)

```
"JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>   End-perform.

If MYTEXT contains less than 500 words and spaces, what happens when you get to
the end? You'll go into an endless loop or abend with 'pointer out of range'. 

Didn't you mean to say, "until MYINDX > 500 or MYPOINTER > length of MYTEXT'?

Suggestion: try 'delimited by ALL SPACE' to avoid all those time-wasting strings
filled with spaces. Change MYINDX to MYINDX-LIMIT so you know how many words
there are, don't have to look at all 500 in subsequent code.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** jzitzelb@tsys.com (Joe Zitzelberger)
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T11:41:17-08:00

```
"JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote in message news:<PrOdnVM-O-kOPdujXTWcjQ@giganews.com>...

>       ...

This syntax has always kinda bothered me.  Do any compiler vendors
offer a <flamebait>sane syntax</flamebait> for this?

My first guess would be to use the function ALL subscripting with
something like:

    Unstring MYTEXT delimited by SPACE into
        MYWORD(ALL)
    End-Unstring

Does the new standard change anything about Unstring?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T13:06:54-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<VpicnYMwc_M5O9ujXTWchw@giganews.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net> <gI2dnXYYs-jB5NijXTWckg@giganews.com> <3E468634.8141B144@netbox.com>`

```

"B Berman" <bberman@netbox.com> wrote in message
news:3E468634.8141B144@netbox.com...
> JerryMouse wrote:
>
> > Here's one example using POINTER for which I haven't been able to find
an
> > easier method to accomplish.
> >
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Not having used pointers before, what does it accomplish in your example ?

It's a replacement for the following:

Unstring MYTEXT delimited by SPACE into
   MYWORD(1)
   MYWORD(2)
   MYWORD(3)
   MYWORD(4)
   MYWORD(5)
   MYWORD(6)
   MYWORD(7)
   MYWORD(8)
   MYWORD(9)
   MYWORD(10)
   MYWORD(11)
   MYWORD(12)
   MYWORD(13)
   MYWORD(14)
   MYWORD(15)
   MYWORD(16)
   MYWORD(17)
   MYWORD(18)
   MYWORD(19)
   MYWORD(20)
   MYWORD(21)
   MYWORD(22)
   MYWORD(23)
   MYWORD(24)
   MYWORD(25)
   MYWORD(26)
   MYWORD(27)
   MYWORD(28)
   MYWORD(29)
   MYWORD(30)
   MYWORD(31)
   MYWORD(32)
   MYWORD(33)
   ...
   MYWORD(500)
   end-unstring

In other words, using the WITH POINTER clause, the unstring command begins
at the byte specified by the pointer variable and increments the pointer as
it moves bytes to the receiving field(s). The delimiter (SPACE in this
example) is counted in the pointer variable but is not moved to the
receiving field.

Here's an example:

MYTEXT = "Now is the time..."

MOVE 1 TO MYPOINTER. {don't forget to initialize to the 1st byte subject to
moving}
Unstring MYTEXT delimited SPACE into
   WORD1
   WORD2
   WORD3
   WORD4 with pointer MYPOINTER.

At the conclusion of the command:
WORD1=Now
WORD2=is
WORD3=the
WORD4=time

Like STRING, there are additional options available.
```

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e46e433.96886824@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net> <gI2dnXYYs-jB5NijXTWckg@giganews.com> <3E468634.8141B144@netbox.com>`

```
B Berman <bberman@netbox.com> wrote:

>JerryMouse wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Not having used pointers before, what does it accomplish in your example ?

COBOL has two interpretations of the word POINTER. Do not confuse this one with
a variable of type (USAGE IS) POINTER. This one is a subscript; the other is a
memory address.

Robert
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@jones0086.freeserve.co.uk (Robert Jones)
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T15:39:13-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<fcd09c56.0302081539.106a4676@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net>`

```
I think that your attempt to write such a book is commendable, but
that you should express gratitude to and not offend those people who
have spent time to produce some quite lengthy constructive
suggestions.  That doesn't mean that you must always agree with them,
but all the contributors so far are very knowledgeable.

> I would if the standards were available electronically. I'm a mobile contract
> programmer who cannot transport a library of paper every time I move, about once
> a year. Why do the cobbler's children have no shoes? 
> 
You can currently get both the 1985 and the 2002 standards from ISO
and ANSI, though the 2002 standard is quite expensive.

> When I'm not certain a feature is in the Standard, I'll change references from
> "the standard" to "standard practice". Free-form is a good example. I've been
> using it since 1984.
> 

I would suggest that you use the term "my recommended practice" as it
would be less misleading to novices.  Your intro could explain that
your recommended practices have been developed from substantial
experience with a variety of organisations and interaction with
colleagues.

I think you should make the distinctions clearer between what is
allowable by the standard and what you personally recommend.  It is OK
for you only to consider a subset of the standards for your
discussion, provided it is not implied that there are are no other
relevant features.  Is is very questionable to recommend implementor
extensions to the standard without making it clear that that is what
they are and which implementors provide them.  Different implementors
may even provide extensions that look similar, but have subtle or even
not so subtle distinctions.

Rober
```

```
Forty years isn't enough? I managed a VSE shop for ten years, where I did heavy
replacement of CICS internals. The 4361, which ran at 1 MIPS, supplied  more
than 100 terminals with .9 second response time, with a standard deviation of
less than .5 sec. You don't get that kinda performance with dead-stock IBM
software.

I'm not down on mainframes so much as the culture surrounding them. Mainframe
programmers are, for the most part, mediocre. In their heyday -- '60s and '70s
-- mainframes attracted bright talent, at least in systems programming.
Nowadays, the bright people are working elsewhere.

>The mainframe world does not move fast for a reason. We'd
>like to think we've learned a few things after all the grief
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>boring thing going on.

Except when management wants something changed NOW, but  IT methodologies such
as CMM and ISO-9000 say it will take 3-6 months. That's a serious disconnect
between IT and the real world. In the last two places where I worked, Accenture
and EDS got fired because their methodologies made changes too slow.

It's all about evolution vs. revolution. I usually come down on the side of
RAPID evolution. Have you read Christopher Alexander's "Notes on the synthesis
of form"? (Harvard Press) Although written by an architect, it's a classic on
the topic of system development.

Robert
```

```
Robert Wagner wrote:
<snip>
> 
> Forty years isn't enough? I managed a VSE shop for ten years, where I did heavy
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> software.
<snip>

Forty years doing the same 6 months of work does not mean
you have 40 years of exposure (if you get my drift).

I've been a software developer, hardware manufacturer's
software writer for two different companies. Then there are
all the companies that I've been on assignment to (as well
as Gubment agencies). 25+ years of programming in COBOL,
RPG, FORTRAN, ALC, BAL, C, C++, as well as several utility
languages. I've worked on 36 bit machines, 16 bit machines,
20 bit machines, 31 bit machines, 32 bit machines and 64 bit
machines.

But what does all of that have to do with the subject at
hand? You are writing a book (and I commend you for that,
and allowing us to crucify your baby) about Best Practices.
But to do this, don't you need to know the number of shops
that do this or that? That use this or that feature but not
another, and why?

There are those who make a religion out of the "GO TO" verb.
Others who made a religion out of ALTER. Then there is the
top down, bottom up, and sideways structured methodologies.

While I might come across as a PIA, the real aim here is to
get all the info you can from curmudgeons like myself to put
out the best book you can. 
<snip>
> I'm not down on mainframes so much as the culture surrounding them. Mainframe
> programmers are, for the most part, mediocre. In their heyday -- '60s and '70s
> -- mainframes attracted bright talent, at least in systems programming.
> Nowadays, the bright people are working elsewhere.
<snip>
<soap box mode>
AMEN! I taught COBOL for a semester. Out of a class of about
50, 10 of them were programmers or would make programmers.
The 25-30 of them couldn't figure out how to write a COBOL
program when 80% of it had been specifically given in the
examples used in the class lectures!

Most of the COBOL programmers that I deal with today would
have been fired before they made it through probation, were
this the mid-'70s thru mid-'80s in the shops where I worked.
And many of the SR. Systems Programmers that I come across
today are what I consider to be JR Systems Programmers. The
best they can do is run SMP/E and a few utility programs.
Very few can explain why the system operates the way it does
(so they can't possibly tune the sucker!).

<\soap box mode>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Liam Devlin <LiamD@optonline.NOSPAM.net>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T06:11:46+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3E45F111.4030405@optonline.NOSPAM.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert Wagner wrote:
> "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

(snip)

>>> ... The sign is over the
>>>low-order digit as 'A' for +1 and 'J' for -1.
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> instructions provided four bits for the length plus sign.

I think you've muddled some things here. The decimal instructions, e.g., 
AP, DP, contain 2 lengths of 4 bits each (there are some exceptions, 
e.g., PACK), limiting the max length of each operand to 16 bytes (you 
move bytes, not digits), giving a maximum of 31 digits plus sign. The 
length codes in instructions are unsigned.

The 15 digits (plus sign) are what fit into 8 bytes. The length code for 
an 8 byte operand is b'0111', which doesn't use all the available bits. 
Multipliers and divisors are limited to 8 bytes.

Here's a snippet of text from the z/Architecture POO:


Packed Format

??????????????????/??????????????????
? D ? D ? D ? D ? ? D ? D ? D ? S ?
??????????????????/??????????????????

In the packed format, each byte contains two decimal digits (D), except 
for the rightmost byte, which contains a sign to the right of a decimal
digit. Decimal arithmetic is performed with operands in the packed 
format and generates results in the packed format. The packed-format 
operands and results of decimal-arithmetic instructions may be up to 16
bytes (31 digits and sign), except that the maximum length of a 
multiplier or divisor is eight bytes (15 digits and sign). In division, 
the sum of the lengths of the quotient and remainder may be from two to 
16 bytes. The editing instructions can fetch as many as 256 decimal 
digits from one or more decimal numbers of variable length, each in the 
packed format.
```

```
Ummm... At this moment, ANSI X3.23-1985 and its two amendments are available
in electronic form from http://www.ansi.org/ for the grand sum of $18.  The
same web site indicates that ISO/IEC 1989:2002 is available in electronic
form from the same source for $237.  There are strong rumors that ANSI will
be distributing the ANSI equivalent of the latter document for a much lower

>

from

You have been using it on machines that supply the feature.  Not all do,
only the ones that you have chosen to work on.  So long as it is not
standard COBOL, it is inappropriate to presume that it is "standard
practice".

> >> The data division has three sections-file, working-storage and
> >> linkage-in which you describe data structures. All three follow the
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> >Ignoring, of course, the fact that you describe the meaty details of
FILEs
> >in the data division...

> They have evaporated over the years. Nowadays, it's fd file-name (period).

FORMAT and EXTERNAL are new for 2002; RECORD clause was enhanced from '74 to
'85; CODE-SET, REPORTS, BLOCK and LINAGE remain from '85.  DATA RECORDS and
VALUE OF were dropped between '85 and 2002, the former because it had been
documentation only and the latter because it described constructs that were
implementor-defined to begin with.  You may choose to avoid these remaining
clauses, but they do have use.

> >and the fact that it also contains the REPORT and
> >COMMUNICATION SECTIONs of '85 COBOL, and the LOCAL-STORAGE and SCREEN
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

Lynch,
> Sears (both mainframe), US Dept of Education (Unix) and other IT shops
large and
> small. As I said in the introduction, this tutorial pares COBOL down to
> essentials by omitting features not used in practice.

Most large-scale Unisys MCP/AS implementations use some flavor of the
Communication Section in practice.  Many Unisys systems have historically
used the standard syntax for their data communications mechanisms.  The
other sections, as I pointed out, are new to the 2002 standard, and may or
may not get much use in future -- but the fact that a brand-new feature is
not yet used much in practice could be attributed to the mere fact that it
is a new feature that has not yet had exposure.

>
> >Lower case is strictly a style issue from '85 COBOL onward; since then,

'02
> >COBOL or earlier compilers that include it as an implementor-defined
> >extension (that fact should be getting pretty clear by now).
>
> We users desire standards to minimize implementor-defined features. We
generally
> avoid features that are different between compilers.

My point exactly.  In free-format source, the appropriate comment indicator
is "*>" according to the standard.

> >> A Cobol string is fixed length, filled with spaces on the right.

No, because it presumes that the space-filling is characteristic of the DATA
ITEM.  It's not; it's a feature of the VERBS that access it.

I did, and I misunderstood your construct.

They are, but they should be interpreted in the light of the fact that ANY
specific format for either one is up to the compiler designer.

Commonly true, but not necessarily so.  Some shops think SIGN SEPARATE is a
better idea for clarity (and ease of transition among character sets).

> The limit of 15 was for packed on a mainframe, because machine language

Maybe on the "mainframe" YOU'RE familiar with.  On the machines I work on,
the limit's 23 digits, and the compiler's extended to allow that limit (with
appropriate warning messages).

> I'll change the text to say packed is efficient on mainframes and possibly
other
> machines designed years ago.

Again, why do you presume nobody could design an efficient packed-decimal
implementation today?

> In practice, Micro Focus, IBM and Fujitsu (at least) use exactly the same
syntax
> in their '85 compilers. That's useful knowledge for the practitioner.

Useful perhaps when you take the position that these are the only compilers,
in any implementation, worth considering.  Those you have indoctrinated to
that effect might have trouble getting jobs in some of our customer's sites
if they expressed such attitudes in their interviews!

> Right. I'll change "are 32-bit" to "are usually 32-bit on today's
machines. In

No, I think you miss the point.  You're proclaiming standard practice
here -- and were proclaiming "standard COBOL" -- when you are actually
describing rules that do NOT apply universally.

> Allocating tables of worst-case size also rests on assumptions about

Your conclusions rest on nonuniversal assumptions as well.
>

However, your assertion was that COMP-1 is *ANSI-standard*.  And your "best

Dunno offhand which "Burroughs", but every MCP/AS implementation I'm aware
of uses x"00" for low-values and x"FF" for high-values in alphanumeric USAGE
DISPLAY contexts.

However, ANSI-85 General Rule 4b3 for MOVE seems to indicate that in MOVE
HIGH-VALUES TO NUMERIC-DISPLAY-ITEM, HIGH-VALUES be interpreted as if it
were a display item containing an integer, which in MCP/AS would treat it as
all "9" characters, and similarly low-values should be equivalent to all
zeroes.  This is a "cross-class" MOVE, of course.

> >> move high-values to a                    *> FF, strings only
> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 6-bit? Has IBM reintroduced the 1401?

No, but Unisys still manufactures, markets and supports a large number of
machines descendant from the Univac 1107, which so far as I know still have
a significant reliance on a 36-bit word length.  They do support 8-bit data,
certainly, but I thought 6-bit was still allowed.

And certainly character-sets requiring more than eight bits are provided
for.


> >> There are two types of perform: in-line loop control and a 'call' to
> >> another paragraph.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> >old is old?

> My comment about calling another paragraph was in the context of PERFORM;
it was
> not in a discussion of the CALL verb.

And it's precisely my point that there's an important semantic distinction.

> I think the comparison is appropriate. Every PERFORM could be replaced by
a CALL
> to a local function. If the function needs access to a file, one must move
the
> file and all operations on it into a single function, because files cannot
be
> passed as parameters.

COULD, but not necessarily SHOULD.  Because it's useful IN SOME CONTEXTS,
IPC was introduced in '74 and significantly amplified in '85 and '02.
Files can be EXTERNAL or GLOBAL in any conformant COBOL since '85.

> >Some machines don't do "static linking" for this.  Neither '74, '85 nor
'02
> >COBOL standards specify how programs in a run unit are linked together.
>
> Agreed. Most compilers do the 'linking' internally, which explains why the
> function must be nested. If the compiler produced an EXTERN, the function
could
> be called by outside programs, thereby violating the spirit of local.
>

This response is meaningful only in your limited experience.  It does not,
for example, make sense to me in the context of Unisys MCP/AS COBOL '85.
The compiler doesn't produce an "EXTERN".


> > > move spaces to input-words
> >> unstring input-line delimited by all space into
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> >Nope.  Standard disallows "ALL" before any sort of literal -- figurative
or
> >otherwise -- in this context.  ALL <dataname>, yes; ALL <literal>, no,
ALL
> >SPACE, no.
>
> You might want to double-check. I do it all the time and it works as
described.

I misread the standard, as Bill Klein diplomatically pointed out offline.
The "ALL" in this case is part of the UNSTRING, not part of the figurative
constant.  UNSTRING BY ALL ALL ... is illegal.  My apologies.

> STRING SPACES, with expressed or implied DELIMITED BY SIZE, will put a
single
> space. One would have to write a loop to clear the receiving string one
> character at a time.
>
> I like the idea of INITIALIZE, but don't use it because it is error-prone.
It
> does not clear FILLERs nor POINTERs. Hopefully, this bug was fixed in the
'02
> standard.

Not a bug, but a specific design decision (in which I was not involved),
along with NOT initializing to whatever is in the VALUE clause, I think to
prevent possible ambiguities and errors.   Your concerns have, I think, been
provided for in '02 COBOL, in which alternatives that include WITH FILLER
and TO VALUE for the record as a whole, and the categories DATA-POINTER,
PROGRAM-POINTER and OBJECT-REFERENCE, have been provided in the expanded
syntax.

> >If you're talking about your example of INSPECT ... CONVERTING, only in
'02
> >COBOL and implementations that have extended their '85 compilers beyond
the
> >applicable standard to include it.
>
> In practice, all major '85 compilers have that feature, and syntax is
identical.

It was you that indicated that all '85-compliant compilers had it.  And
there are more than a few customers who would challenge the premise that any
'85-compliant compiler that does NOT have this feature is a minor compiler!

> It was an IBM mainframe COBOL invention which has been available since the
days
> of '68 COBOL. Micro Focus and Realia copied it.

It was an IBM mainframe EXTENSION to standard COBOL, and it is expressly
identified as such in my copy of an old IBM manual (file #S360-24, order
#GC28-6394-4, "IBM DOS Full American National Standard COBOL Version 3").
Micro Focus and Realia may have copied it, but not everyone did, or had a
particular reason to.

What is "standard practice" on IBM and IBM-inspired COBOL compilers may not
be "standard practice" when your vision is broadened to include other
traditions and environments.

I'm afraid your pontifications about what is "standard practice" could be
regarded as perhaps parochial, which is not exactly your intent.   Certainly
the blanket substitution of "standard practice" over "standard COBOL" is an
improvement, for as I've shown the latter is by and large misleading at best
and often flat wrong.  But I still believe a blanket "caveat" at the
beginning of your book as to the list of implementations to which your "best
practices" are applicable would be a wise addition, as would an indication
that no denigration of any compiler or implementation in which your "best
practices" in fact produce suboptimal results, encounter syntax errors as
coded, or corrupt valuable customer data is intended or implied!

     -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T17:16:10-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b29isq$1rmr$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net> <b28u4t$1dsk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e483ff6.185926688@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3e483ff6.185926688@news.optonline.net...

> We don't print REPORTs any longer, we present them on the screen.
>

We who?  "Select ... assign to printer..." was alive and kicking last I saw
... So was REPORTS ARE ...

> SCREEN SECTION has been in the standard since '85. If the '02 version is
text
> mode, like '85, forget it. Everyone wants GUI screens.

Nope, SCREEN SECTION was introduced in '02.  But toward the end of the
standards process there was some significant (and heated) opposition to it
precisely on the grounds you state; it is in fact one of those parts of the
standard that is under the category "processor dependent".

Note that the sentence "Factors that may be considered include, but are not
limited to, hardware capability, software capability, and market
poisitioning of the processor." that appears in the functional definition of
"processor dependent" was the subject of long and heated debate both during
the standards process as well as many discussions right here in this very
forum.

> >> >Are you sure *ALL* Pascal implementations comply with this?
> >>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> No again. Null-terminated strings do not. I'll change it to "strings with
an
> explicit length".

Are you sure all "strings with an explicit length" describe them with four
ASCII characters representing decimal digits, the last having the high-order
four bits indicating whether the length is positive or negative?  Why can't
I describe the length of a string "PICTURE S9(11) BINARY EXTENDED", or for
that matter "USAGE DOUBLE" if I feel like it?   If your variable-length
strings are coming from elsewhere, there's no telling what the format will
be!

> You are picky about semantics.  I like your Socratic approach to pointing
out
> errors.

Working on the standards, and working on the documentation for the compilers
I support, has made me decidedly picky about semantics.

> What an odd number. It's not a power of two.

Actually it's related to a power of two.  The relevant value is 2**78 - 1,
which when represented in decimal is 302,231,454,903,657,293,676,543,
obviously a 24-digit number.  Since numbers larger than that can't be
represented in this context *as an integer*, the largest number of DECIMAL
digits we allow is 23.

> Because there is little demand for it.

Yet.  "Standard arithmetic" in '02-standard COBOL is defined in decimal
terms (though it doesn't have to be implemented *using* decimal arithmetic),
and the idea that programs can be written in COBOL and their arithmetic
algorithms, no matter how complex, be directly portable without change to
any arbitrary platform is I think a Really Neat Feature of the '02 standard.
Arithmetic in all prior standards is implementor-defined; that's not the
case for standard arithmetic.  And it's not a "processor-dependent" feature,
either.  It's mandatory for conformant compilers.  I'd expect, once the
content of the standard gets more widely publicized, this is going to be one
of the hotly-demanded features from the user community for the very reason
that the user's investment in algorithms is protected in ways that were
never the case before.  A packed-decimal mantissa is a perfectly reasonable
way of ensuring conformance with these decimal-based rules.

> I hate using the linker to pass addressability. That's what parameters are
for.

Who said anything about a linker?  I certainly didn't.  Unisys MCP/AS (ex
Burroughs Large System) COBOL85 does not use its linker (a product called
BINDER) for ANY ANSI-standard implementations (IPC-related or otherwise) so
far as I know.  All such issues, if unresolved during the compilation
process itself, are handled by the system at run time.

   -Chuck Stevens
```

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T23:13:15-08:00

- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302102313.79654448@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <b21k1c$2lsc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e453b28.243625521@news.optonline.net> <b28u4t$1dsk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3e483ff6.185926688@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> LOCAL-STORAGE has been available on Micro Focus for years. It's the stack, so
> beloved to C programmers. One uses it to make a program reentrant and recursive.
> I haven't seen anyone using it because these issues are above the domain of the
> language. They are Operating System issues. 

Local-Storage, and several other features are there for writing
Windows API programs and not for pretending that it is C.

> SCREEN SECTION has been in the standard since '85. 

No, wrong.  SCREEN SECTION was never part of the '85 standard. 

Get a set a MicroFocus manuals for a recent product, well anytime in
the 15 years, and look up these items.  If a syntax item is in a box
marked (MF) or (OSVS) or (XOPEN) or similar then it is _NOT_ part of
the ANSI Cobol '85 standard.

Or look in the reserved word list.  SCREEN is marked XOPEN, MF3, MS2
and not ANS85.

> If the '02 version is text
> mode, like '85, forget it. Everyone wants GUI screens. 

C doesn't define a GUI screen, nor do C++, Pascal, Python, PERL, or a
whole raft of other languages.  They all use GUI via add-on libraries.
 Cobolk can do exactly the same.
 
> So what do they do when they think nobody's looking? They
> define EVERYthing global. 

Why do you always use hyperbole in every statement that you make ?

The problem with hyperbole is that anyone with two clues to rub
together will know that some C programmers will use extern for some
variables.

When you say "they [all] define EVERYthing global" this is obviously
not true. Some is defined global, some is module local, some is
function local, some is block local.

These gross exagerations erode your credability, eventually many
people simply discount whatever you say.

Of course your credability is not helped when you persistently say
something is 'standard'/'not standard' after it is repeatedly pointed
out you are wrong.
```

#### ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-07T23:09:48-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> * Use the free-form option of your compiler and write in lower case.

I dislike all lower case as much as I dislike all upper case. Use of
Modula/2 style with all upper COBOL key words and mixed case
(capitalised) user words gives an appearance that I prefer and, with
mild colour hightlighting in the editor, misspelt key words are easy
to see. I also dislike dictatorial statements.
 
> * Write in a consistent style in order to facilitate searching source
> code. For example, when you want to search 25 source modules to find
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> waste time skipping references which do not modify it.

 ... or 'SET foo TO' or 'MOVE .. TO bar foo' 

I have no problem grepping to a file and then scanning this to find
all required references.

> * Use linked lists rather than arrays. Lists use only the memory
> required yet are nearly unlimited in size. Arrays wastefully allocate
> the worst case, then are limited to that size. 

What particular _COBOL_ mechanism were you intending to use for a
linked list that does dynamic allocation ?

> There are two useful Cobol file types: line sequential (aka ASCII text
> file) and indexed. Nobody uses relative and record sequential formats.

Yet another hyperbole.  Relative files are very fast compared to
indexed and can be useful, for example for linked record sets.

> If an operation fails, file-status will be non-zero. 

If an operation is successful this is indicated in the _first_byte_ of
the file status.  The second byte may contain additional information.

That is the file-status may be non-zero on a _successful_ operation.

> The most common codes are 95 file not found on open, 

Maybe on your system, but that is not a code on mine, and anyway file
not found has a standard code of "39".

> 10 end of file (not an error) and
> 22 insert failed because record already exists. You should test this
> after every file operation.

What about "23" for missing record ? isn't that common ?  On my
system, '9D' is fairly common - record locked.
 
> Fujitsu uses Btrieve for its indexed file system. The other compilers
> use proprietary formats. 

Fujitsu _can_ use BTrieve, but doesn't normally, BTrieve is also a
proprietry format.

> Because it is an ANSI Standard
> language, SQL is exactly the same on all relational databases. 

How pathetically optimistic of you.  There certianly is a common
subset that can be relied on to be portable but all SQLs have their
own extensions and restrictions.

> If your
> SQL works on Sybase, it will work on Oracle and Informix with no
> changes. 

Maybe, maybe not.

> Do not roll your own bubble sort. It is at least 50 times slower than
> any other algorithm. 

Nonsense.  Bubble sorts can be faster than others on small arrays, say
less than  200 items.  More complex sorts are only worth while for
larger numbers of items.

>  Do not insert records into an indexed file or
> database just to sort them.

SORT has a limitation in that is has no equal key procedure. Sorts
which can collapse by adding equal keys together can be _much_ faster
by using an indexed file and updating or inserting records as
required.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-08T20:17:35+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
<snip>
>> * Write in a consistent style in order to facilitate searching source
>> code. For example, when you want to search 25 source modules to find
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>all required references.

'TO bar foo' will be found by a regular expression search. If foo is a pointer
and you consistantly use SET, or consistantly use MOVE, you can find lines which
change it. 

>> * Use linked lists rather than arrays. Lists use only the memory
>> required yet are nearly unlimited in size. Arrays wastefully allocate
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>linked list that does dynamic allocation ?

All mainstream compilers come with an allocate function. Micro Focus calls it
CBL_ALLOC_MEM (note CBL in the name); Fujitsu uses LocalAlloc; Realia used to
use DOS-MEM-ALLOC, now probably WIN-MEM-ALLOC.

>> There are two useful Cobol file types: line sequential (aka ASCII text
>> file) and indexed. Nobody uses relative and record sequential formats.
>
>Yet another hyperbole.  Relative files are very fast compared to
>indexed and can be useful, for example for linked record sets.

The problem with relative files is finding a record. Hashing produces synonyms,
which cause extra reads and/or sparsely filled files. Moreover, hashing is not
scaleable. An algorithm which works on 10,000 records will cause an unacceptable
number of synonyms with presented with 100,000. 

Indexed can handle linked records too, with the additional benefit they might be
close by, if you design keys that way, therefore already in a cache. Relative
files are usually not good at positioning related records close together on
disk.

>> If an operation fails, file-status will be non-zero. 
>
>If an operation is successful this is indicated in the _first_byte_ of
>the file status.  The second byte may contain additional information.
>That is the file-status may be non-zero on a _successful_ operation.

Correct. The only non-zero I've seen is 01 for duplicate secondary key.

>> The most common codes are 95 file not found on open, 
>
>Maybe on your system, but that is not a code on mine, and anyway file
>not found has a standard code of "39".

Actually, there is no Standard for file not found. 

>> 10 end of file (not an error) and
>> 22 insert failed because record already exists. You should test this
>> after every file operation.
>
>What about "23" for missing record ? isn't that common ?  

Read produces 23; start/read next does not. I didn't show read in chapter 8.

> On my system, '9D' is fairly common - record locked.

Good catch. I'll add that. Again, codes beginning with 9 are non-standard. 

>> Fujitsu uses Btrieve for its indexed file system. The other compilers
>> use proprietary formats. 
>
>Fujitsu _can_ use BTrieve, but doesn't normally, BTrieve is also a
>proprietry format.

Another good catch. Thanks.

<snip>

>>  Do not insert records into an indexed file or
>> database just to sort them.
>
>SORT has a limitation in that is has no equal key procedure. 

In some environments, COBOL calls an external, third-party sort which provides
an option to add equal keys. You activate it by saying WITH SORT OPTION
'sum=(a,b), ...' Of course, the option is different for each sort program.

If that is not available, you can buffer a few hundred records during the input
procedure, look for duplicates yourself, retaining records with a high hit count
and/or recent hits, flushing the buffer into the sort at end of input.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T11:36:41+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

>>What particular _COBOL_ mechanism were you intending to use for a
>>linked list that does dynamic allocation ?
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> used to use DOS-MEM-ALLOC, now probably WIN-MEM-ALLOC.

But it ain't Cobol.  POINTER is an extension in Cobol'85 and the use of 
them may be different, or not implemented at all, on current compilers.  

Dynamic allocation is not standard and is OS and compiler dependant.

The reason that Cobol has not added dynamic allocation is because it is 
dangerous and very error prone if the programmer is left to do it and is 
best avoided for all production work.

Also you are quite wrong if you think that each malloc and free will aquire 
and release memory, a heap is usually aquired of some arbitraily large size 
for the program when the first malloc is done and another is aquired when 
that has been used up.  These are not released until the program terminates 
(or, possibly, with Windows, never).
 
There is also _no_ guarantee, as you seem to assume, that accessing a 
linked list in dynamic memory is in any way faster than array access. It 
may be on some systems but not on others.

>>Yet another hyperbole.  Relative files are very fast compared to
>>indexed and can be useful, for example for linked record sets.
 
> The problem with relative files is finding a record. Hashing produces

I did say 'linked record sets'.  These are like linked lists, but on disk.  
The master record (say in an indexed file) has the relatative record number 
of the first in the linked chain (and possibley the last) and each linked 
record points to the next.

> synonyms, which cause extra reads and/or sparsely filled files. Moreover,
> hashing is not scaleable. An algorithm which works on 10,000 records will
> cause an unacceptable number of synonyms with presented with 100,000.

So ?  One just repacks the hashed file every so often into a larger space 
and uses a dynamic hashing that takes the file size into account.

Pick and derivitives use hashed files and they work fine, and very fast.
 
> Indexed can handle linked records too, with the additional benefit they
> might be close by, if you design keys that way, 

I think that you have an entirelky optimistic view of how records might be 
stored in an indexed file.  They only _appear_ to be close because a 
sequential read will fetch them in key order, they may actually be 
scattered across the disk in entirely random way, especially on a very 
active file where the system will reuse space from deleted records.

When a new record is written it is put into the first avilable space large 
enough for it, or added to the end in roughly chronolical order.

> therefore already in a cache. 

Pity this does not match reality.

> Relative files are usually not good at positioning related records
> close together on disk.

Relative files will [usually] write records physically based on their 
relative key.  It is very easy to ensure that a block of linked records are 
physically grouped and therefore cached together.

You simply have your assertions reversed (for any system that I use).
 
> Correct. The only non-zero I've seen is 01 for duplicate secondary key.

That is 02 actually.  04, 05 and 07 are also defined.
 
> Actually, there is no Standard for file not found.

   35   An OPEN operation with the I-O, INPUT or EXTEND phrases has been
        tried on a non-OPTIONAL file that is not present.

   05   The referenced optional file is not present at the time the
        OPEN statement is executed.


>>SORT has a limitation in that is has no equal key procedure.
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> program.

and is not Cobol.
 
> If that is not available, you can buffer a few hundred records during the
> input procedure, look for duplicates yourself, retaining records with a
> high hit count and/or recent hits, flushing the buffer into the sort at
> end of input.

Yuk, what a crap way of doing it.  Just write an indexed file and get over 
it.  Your code could still be slower because of all the array (or linked 
list) scanning and beacuse the isam file will be cached anyway.

One (of the many) problems with your 'buffer few hundred records' is that 
you will still wind up with multiple records for some keys and thus will 
have to code the merging of these in the read.

Cobol is for _business_ programming, doing a job, not seeing how many 
clever lines of code tyou can write.
```

```
Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

This is FUD (fear, uncertainty and doubt). In practice, it is implemented
exactly the same on all mainstream compilers: Micro Focus, IBM and Fujitsu. It
is real-world COBOL, even though you don't like it.
 
>Dynamic allocation is not standard and is OS and compiler dependant.

No. The functions supplied with the COBOL compiler make it transparent to the
OS.

>The reason that Cobol has not added dynamic allocation is because it is 
>dangerous and very error prone if the programmer is left to do it and is 
>best avoided for all production work.

The reason why COBOL has not added dynamic allocation is programmer timidity,
based on previous failures. I use it routinely in production work with no
problem. If you look at holdings of any mutual fund on the Web, they came
through my code. Did you see run times of the demo? Eight seconds to read, parse
and analyze 88K lines is less than one millisecond per line. I challenge you to
best that with your techniques.

>Also you are quite wrong if you think that each malloc and free will aquire 
>and release memory, a heap is usually aquired of some arbitraily large size 
>for the program when the first malloc is done and another is aquired when 
>that has been used up.  These are not released until the program terminates 
>(or, possibly, with Windows, never).

Fujitsu has heap-oriented functions, which I bypassed. They have problems with
garbage collection and size limitation (same as arrays). I just want a chunk of
memory to call my own until I release it  .. or terminate. 

>There is also _no_ guarantee, as you seem to assume, that accessing a 
>linked list in dynamic memory is in any way faster than array access. It 
>may be on some systems but not on others.

So run a speed test. Don't make it a 'religious' issue. After you find lists are
IN FACT faster, what will you do? Change your style?

>Pick and derivitives use hashed files and they work fine, and very fast.

ROTFL. The only programming language on Pick is interpreted Basic. Do you want
to talk about Real Programming?

>> Indexed can handle linked records too, with the additional benefit they
>> might be close by, if you design keys that way, 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>active file where the system will reuse space from deleted records.

They will be close after reorganizing the file. Production files are reorganized
routinely.

<snip>

>> If that is not available, you can buffer a few hundred records during the
>> input procedure, look for duplicates yourself, retaining records with a
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>clever lines of code tyou can write.

In Texas, the low-brow approach is usually pronounced 'bidness'. 

I've worked in _business_ for 40 years. The logical problems presented by
business are no different from other domains, nor their solutions. I've used the
'buffer a few hundred records'  approach many times with good results. 

The shop where I work now has a program which sorts a file by inserting into an
indexed file. I'm itching to change it. Will let you know when I have the
authority, and how much faster it runs. Probably >95% faster.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> >> All mainstream compilers come with an allocate function. Micro Focus calls
> >> it CBL_ALLOC_MEM (note CBL in the name); Fujitsu uses LocalAlloc; Realia
> >> used to use DOS-MEM-ALLOC, now probably WIN-MEM-ALLOC.

> This is FUD (fear, uncertainty and doubt). In practice, it is implemented
> exactly the same on all mainstream compilers: Micro Focus, IBM and Fujitsu. It
> is real-world COBOL, even though you don't like it.

Fujitsu has BASED-STORAGE SECTION, has the ADDR() function. 
Microfocus utilises LINKAGE SECTION and SET pointer TO ADDRESS OF name
and vv.

It may well be that one could contrive a common subset that may work
accross some compilers.  Your 'exactly the same' is just not true.
  
> >Dynamic allocation is not standard and is OS and compiler dependant.
> 
> No. The functions supplied with the COBOL compiler make it transparent to the
> OS.

Dynamic allocation is not standard and is .. complier depentent: you
say yourself that the routines above are different for each compiler.

 .. is OS dependent: you say yourself that Realia uses DOS-MEM-ALLOC
or WIN-MEM-ALLOC depending on the OS.  Fujitsu _on_Windows_ uses the
_Windows_ call LocalAlloc. On other operating systems using Fujitsu
compilers the programmer would also use the specific memory allocater,
one of the malloc() family.

> The reason why COBOL has not added dynamic allocation is programmer timidity,
> based on previous failures. 

One of the features of C++ over C is that the classes have
constructors and destructors to take away from the programmer the need
to deal with memory allocation directly and to reduce the opportunity
to introduce bugs.

With Java they went one step further with garbage collection because
programmers can't be trusted to get it right, or in fact because it
just takes too long to program it correctly compared with other
mechanims.

Cobol got its stance correct, other languages are moving away from low
level grubbiness with direct machine addresses by restricting access
to them or disguising them.

> I use it routinely in production work with no problem.

Maybe, but you are recommending them for general use and not all
programmers are as clever or experienced as you obviously are.

> Fujitsu has heap-oriented functions, which I bypassed. They have problems with
> garbage collection and size limitation (same as arrays). I just want a chunk of
> memory to call my own until I release it  .. or terminate. 

So you avoided the compiler provided routines and went directly to the
OS for memory.  This will still give you a heap of some arbitrary size
to be used for several allocations of memory and the individual parts
won't be released until the end of the process, but they may be
reused.

Memory allocation always includes an overhead, in some cases
significant.
 
> So run a speed test. Don't make it a 'religious' issue. 

Well actually it was you that made it a religious issue with your:

  "The linked list should be your first choice when the list contains
a
   variable number of things. 
   and  access is extremely fast because stepping to the next entry 
   requires only changing a pointer. An array, by contrast, requires a
   multiplication on each subscripted reference."

which is a doctrinal statement as if it were carved in stone.

You haven't just suggested that it be one tool amonst many, you have
advocated that it _should_ be used, before even considering others. 
Almost all arrays contain a variable number of things.

You haven't cared about the allocation overhead, that array indexes
_may_ be implemented as machine addresses, or that the dynamic memory
_may_ be distributed in memory and parts _may_ be subject to being
rolled out to virtual memory.

> After you find lists are IN FACT faster, 

Whether they are faster or not will depend on many factors.  For
example if the data in an array is in sorted order a SEARCH ALL will
be _much_faster_ than a serial scan of your list.  If there are few
traversals of an array the alloc overhead may outweigh any
multiplication overheads.

> what will you do? Change your style?

No, I'll keep with the modern trend of moving away from manipulating
memory addresses.  Is the purpose of your document to get people to
change to doing it _your_ way ?

> >Pick and derivitives use hashed files and they work fine, and very fast.
> 
> ROTFL. The only programming language on Pick is interpreted Basic. Do you want
> to talk about Real Programming?

Is that a religious issue for you too ?

> They will be close after reorganizing the file. Production files are 
> reorganized routinely.

So you achieve your 'savings' (if they are such) by applying an
overhead.  In order to get the records into close proximity it
requires a physical sequential unload of the data and a reload, not
just an index reorganization.

Loading an indexed file in key order may get the data records
physically adjacent but may not give the best index optimisation,
requiring too many rotations.

In any case your hypothetic advantage of clustered records is just as
available in relative files and so is not an advantage for indexed
files at all.

> In Texas, the low-brow approach is usually pronounced 'bidness'. 

In Texas there are many strange beliefs and practices.
 
> The shop where I work now has a program which sorts a file by inserting 
> into an indexed file. I'm itching to change it. Will let you know when 
> I have the authority, and how much faster it runs. Probably >95% faster.

Another opportunity to show how clever you can be, well done.  Of
course it may take a decade or two for the savings to compensate for
the cost of your time in developing this instead of doing something
useful, but you may as well make sure that all the following
developers in the site have something to wonder at as they stubble
through it trying to defeat its cleverness.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>and vv.

That's what Fujitsu documentation says. I believed it for awhile, until I tried
the industry-standard way and found it worked. I documented this in the paper:
"Fujitsu: A good compiler, whose weaknesses are ... incorrect documentation of
pointer operations, ...". Look at the Demo. It was compiled under Fujitsu v3 and
it works. It doesn't have any BASED-STORAGE nor FUNCTION ADDR().

>It may well be that one could contrive a common subset that may work
>accross some compilers.  Your 'exactly the same' is just not true.

Yes it is.

> .. is OS dependent: you say yourself that Realia uses DOS-MEM-ALLOC
>or WIN-MEM-ALLOC depending on the OS.  Fujitsu _on_Windows_ uses the
>_Windows_ call LocalAlloc. On other operating systems using Fujitsu
>compilers the programmer would also use the specific memory allocater,
>one of the malloc() family.

It's true that the function name is different for each compiler. Call malloc()
in the C library would work on EVERY compiler and OS, but you excluded that
option by asking for a COBOL solution. I came up with a compromise solution in
the Demo -- a COBOL function named malloc. When moving to a new platform, you
need to change only a single line of code, and thousands of programs calling the
guy will work as before. 

>One of the features of C++ over C is that the classes have
>constructors and destructors to take away from the programmer the need
>to deal with memory allocation directly and to reduce the opportunity
>to introduce bugs.

Well said. Most of us are not using OO COBOL, so I proposed an 'almost OO'
approach under the '85 spec and readily available COBOL industry practices.

>Cobol got its stance correct, other languages are moving away from low
>level grubbiness with direct machine addresses by restricting access
>to them or disguising them.

Realia tried that by denying you access to the pointer. You'd call using a
structure in LINKAGE SECTION, it would get a pointer and set it as the base
'under the covers'. You couldn't step on the pointer. Problem is, you couldn't
build linked lists. 

>> I use it routinely in production work with no problem.
>
>Maybe, but you are recommending them for general use and not all
>programmers are as clever or experienced as you obviously are.

I prefer 'skilled' over 'clever'.

>So you avoided the compiler provided routines and went directly to the
>OS for memory.  This will still give you a heap of some arbitrary size
>to be used for several allocations of memory and the individual parts
>won't be released until the end of the process, but they may be
>reused.

The Demo frees structures at the end of each source program. Watching it running
with a performance monitor shows a pretty wave-like motion in allocated memory. 

>Memory allocation always includes an overhead, in some cases
>significant.

It's lightning fast on Sun Solaris, and not too shabby on NT 4 either. On
Windows, the problem is not slow allocation but rather high paging rate. 

>You haven't just suggested that it be one tool amonst many, you have
>advocated that it _should_ be used, before even considering others. 
>Almost all arrays contain a variable number of things.

How many tools do you need for a list of identical objects? Hold on, more coming


>You haven't cared about the allocation overhead, that array indexes
>_may_ be implemented as machine addresses, or that the dynamic memory
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>multiplication overheads.

You put too much emphasis on allocation overhead. That's not the problem. The
problem is memory management AFTER the structures are created. As I said, Unix
and NT handle it well, Win-9x does not. 

>> what will you do? Change your style?
>
>No, I'll keep with the modern trend of moving away from manipulating
>memory addresses.  Is the purpose of your document to get people to
>change to doing it _your_ way ?

Yes. It's a tutorial intended to show novices how to program well within the
constraints of COBOL '85 as implemented today. It's not about theoretical
computer science. I'm a practitioner. 

>> >Pick and derivitives use hashed files and they work fine, and very fast.
>> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Is that a religious issue for you too ?

Yes, Real Programming is a religious issue for me. C++, Java, et al. are
attempts at real programming. Lengthy discussions about them are not appropriate
in this COBOL forum, but brief mentions are within the spirit. Interpreted
Basic, on the other hand, is amateur hour. 

FWIW, I saw Dick Pick's car in Dallas. It had a vanity license plate reading
PICK.

>> They will be close after reorganizing the file. Production files are 
>> reorganized routinely.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>just an index reorganization.

As I said, production files are routinely reorganized.

>> The shop where I work now has a program which sorts a file by inserting 
>> into an indexed file. I'm itching to change it. Will let you know when 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>through it trying to defeat its cleverness.

You are using "clever" as a negative adjective, often associated with obfuscated
and hard to understand. The thrust of my work is opposite from that, to make
code clear and understandable. Look at the Demo. Tell me what you find "clever"
and I'll rewrite it.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T19:31:20-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b26vd9$qhf$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3e45ab96.16845787@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302091235.63733b5@posting.google.com> <3e46f9ed.102449473@news.optonline.net>`

```
<latest thread portions snipped>

In case anyone is interested in "portable" application controlled storage
management, you might want to ask your "compiler vendor of choice"
(mainframe, Unix, workstation, whatever) when/if they plan on implementing
the ALLOCATE and FREE statements as they are defined in the 2002 COBOL
Standard (along with both USAGE POINTER and PROCEDURE-POINTER).   Along with
the TYPDEF, TYPE, and SAME AS  data division "attributes," these may (if
widely implemented) provide significant portable functionality for those who
*care about* storage management at the lowest levels.  If, on the other
hand, you are interested in "commonly understood"  and easily maintained by
the "average COBOL programmer" - you probably want to avoid some of these
features "like the plague".

In my opinion (and this is just a VERY PERSONAL opinion), all the
discussions comparing

 INSPECT vs byte-table processing
    or
 FUNCTION REVERSE vs single byte searches
   or

any other set of comparisons between "coding techniques" where it takes
100's of thousands of executions of single code constructs to show any
significant performance differences are *in MOST cases* not worth worry
about.  This doesn't mean that you should design "sloppy" algorithms - but
it  does mean that specific statement comparisons USUALLY aren't worth
"tricky" code portions that *might* be hard to maintain by some other
programmer "called in at 3am" to fix a bug.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T17:48:20+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b27b3v$e4k$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3e46f9ed.102449473@news.optonline.net> <b26vd9$qhf$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <3e4727d4.114201965@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

> Applause for ALLOCATE and FREE. I take back everything negative I said
> about standards committees.
 
> Let's not denegrate average COBOL programmers. Old-timers who prefix
> paragraph names with 4-5 digit numbers and cannot understand NOT .. have
> at it. But there is a new generation.

Of course there are also old-timer C programmers from the 70s who manually 
allocate memory and release it, the last 20 years with C++ have made that 
archaic in the C world.

The actual reason for LOCAL-STORAGE, POINTER, PROCEDURE-POINTER, and other 
esoteric items is not so that Cobol can pretend to be C from the early 
1980s, but so that it can interface to so-called Operating Systems (eg 
Windows) that were designed around the early 80s requiring the use of these 
assembler level 'features'.

Just because you _can_ do what C programmers thought was really cool a 
quarter century ago, doesn't mean that it is 'good thing'.

I recall a novice Cobol programmer who had just done a C course at school 
asking "how can I do pointers and other advanced things like C does".  
Because the class that he learnt C's pointers was called 'advanced 
programming' he thought this meant that pointers were an 'advanced 
feature'.  But no, pointers are actually a very fundemental feature such as 
are needed in assembler and machine level programming, it was an 'advanced' 
course because you don't want novices playing around with them, or with 
razor blades.

Of course with modern languages, advanced programmers don't get to play 
with pointers either.

> Having worked in production support, where 400,000 people wouldn't get
> paid unless the problem was fixed NOW, I know that problems are seldom in
> source code. They're usually in transaction records. Delete the offending
> record and the system runs.

ROTFL, "It's the data!!!".  NO, it's the program not being defensive.  The 
only program that _must_ have clean data is one that doesn't have users 
supplying the data.
```

```
Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>Robert Wagner wrote:
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>quarter century ago, doesn't mean that it is 'good thing'.

Very well said You caused me to have second thoughts about advocating linked
lists. But (you knew a but was coming) we're talking about COBOL '85, not OO
COBOL. Our only alternatives are arrays and lists. Like C programmers in the
'70s, who were faced with the same choice, we find advantages in lists. The
biggest advantage is (practically) unlimited size. Another is variable-length
entries, which are good when dealing with text records and words, as the Demo
does. 

The other alternative is OO COBOL. Your well presented argument convinced me to
seriously consider that. 

Robert
```

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> >It may well be that one could contrive a common subset that may work
> >accross some compilers.  Your 'exactly the same' is just not true.
> 
> Yes it is.

It is only 'exactly the same' for the common subset of some compilers.

> It's true that the function name is different for each compiler. Call malloc()
> in the C library would work on EVERY compiler and OS, 

No.  Not every compiler produces code that can inter-operate with a C
library. It is not just malloc() that is required but a C runtime and
this may clash with some Cobol run-times.  Granted that most newer
compilers are becoming more C friendly it is hyperbole to claim 'EVERY
compiler and OS' until you have actually demonstrated this.

> Well said. Most of us are not using OO COBOL, so I proposed an 'almost OO'
> approach under the '85 spec and readily available COBOL industry practices.

But it is nowhere near 'almost OO', it is 'machine orientated'.  OO is
what is replacing your approach.

> Realia tried that by denying you access to the pointer. You'd call using a
> structure in LINKAGE SECTION, it would get a pointer and set it as the base
> 'under the covers'. You couldn't step on the pointer. Problem is, you couldn't
> build linked lists. 

Perhaps they knew why they did this.

> >Maybe, but you are recommending them for general use and not all
> >programmers are as clever or experienced as you obviously are.
> 
> I prefer 'skilled' over 'clever'.

It was meant as an insult    ;-)

> It's lightning fast on Sun Solaris, and not too shabby on NT 4 either. On
> Windows, the problem is not slow allocation but rather high paging rate. 

That seems to be a backdown from your previous stance that dynamic was
always IN FACT faster.

> You put too much emphasis on allocation overhead. That's not the problem. The
> problem is memory management AFTER the structures are created. As I said, Unix
> and NT handle it well, Win-9x does not. 

But a static array will use as much or more memory.  In fact malloc()
or equivalent is usually OK if it is used with requests and free()
that are of the same size.  If random sizes are used (such as with
text of various line lengths) then the heap memory gets fragmented
after a while and slows down considerably as it searches through
free()ed blocks looking for the right (or close) size.

> Yes. It's a tutorial intended to show novices how to program well within the
> constraints of COBOL '85 as implemented today. 

If you wanted to show _novices_ how to program in C or in Cobol you
would keep right away from pointers and malloc().

> Interpreted Basic, on the other hand, is amateur hour. 

There are many significant professional systems implemented in Pick
which is a fast and reliable database with full featured scripting
languages.  The reason that it has lasted 40 years is that, like
COBOL, it provides reliable business systems.  Just because BASIC has
been given a bad name by poor implementations such as MicroSoft's
doen't mean that it is amateur.
 
> FWIW, I saw Dick Pick's car in Dallas. It had a vanity license plate reading
> PICK.
 
That must have been a whaile ago, he's been dead for a decade.

> You are using "clever" as a negative adjective, 

Quite.

> often associated with obfuscated
> and hard to understand. The thrust of my work is opposite from that, to make
> code clear and understandable. 

Everyone thinks that their own code is 'clear and understandable',
that is because they recognise it so readily and thus need no study to
know what it will do.  After 40 years of course you instantly and with
perfect clarity see every nuance and implication of your own code.

When you look at other's code, however, you will think that it is not
so clear and understandable as your own.  You have to translate the
idiom, remember what various user words mean in terms of your own
usage, search instead of looking to the expected place.

> Look at the Demo. Tell me what you find "clever"
> and I'll rewrite it.

    ;-)
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

both COBOL and C compile to machine language. If you're running COBOL under an
interpreter, this whole topic is moot because you obviously don't care about
speed. 

>But it is nowhere near 'almost OO', it is 'machine orientated'.  OO is
>what is replacing your approach.

>> It's lightning fast on Sun Solaris, and not too shabby on NT 4 either. On

>always IN FACT faster.

We have the results from one OS and will have a second tomorrow, after it stops
snowing in New York.

>But a static array will use as much or more memory. 

It doesn't USE more memory, it allocates more memory. There is a difference. The
memory manager should swap out the unused portion. If doesn't, paging can kill
performance. When running the Demo on a smallish machine (48M) under Win-9x,
there were 350 page-ins per second, sustained. That's a BAD memory manager, and
the same one used for the first comparison benchmark.

>In fact malloc()
>or equivalent is usually OK if it is used with requests and free()
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>free()ed blocks looking for the right (or close) size.

Not if they are freed in the reverse of the order they were acquired. The memory
manager will coalesce the little areas into a single big one. I do that in the
Demo. A comment reads:
*> To prevent fragmentation, resources such as memory and files should be
*> released in the reverse order they were acquired. 

>> FWIW, I saw Dick Pick's car in Dallas. It had a vanity license plate reading
>> PICK.
> 
>That must have been a whaile ago, he's been dead for a decade.

>usage, search instead of looking to the expected place.

I've worked on C code that I thought much better than my own. I've never seen
COBOL that I thought was better. Only McCracken comes close.

I hate to admit it, but C attracts better programmers than COBOL does.

Robert
```
