```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> Most C compilers don't require a runtime just to call the OS API. 

malloc() and free() and their variations are not system calls (OS
API), they are part of the C runtime library.  On *nix they use the
brk() system call to obtain some large block of memory for the heap.

> I'm assuming both COBOL and C compile to machine language. 

Thay might do, but they might compile to machine code objects that are
incompatible with some C runtime.  There may be many reasons for this.

Of course when you only have a single vendor supplying all languages
(such as mainframes with IBM then you expect them to interoperate. 
However most Cobol vendors have rather smaller focus.

> If you're running COBOL under an
> interpreter, this whole topic is moot because you obviously don't care about
> speed. 

In most cases where Cobol is used for business applications the
processing requirements are not particularly heavy compared to the
file I-O costs.  I have run a 8085 2.5MHz based MP/M multiuser system
with 3 users running MF Level II Cobol in interpreted mode (.int).  On
measuring the free CPU time this indicated that mostly the system was
waiting for disk transfers.

It is not that users of interpreted code 'don't care about speed', but
simply that for all practical purposes it makes bugger all difference.
 
> We have the results from one OS and will have a second tomorrow, after it stops
> snowing in New York.

Meanwhile I sit on the beach watching the America's Cup yachts
practicing, racing starts on Saturday.

> >But a static array will use as much or more memory. 
> 
> It doesn't USE more memory, it allocates more memory. There is a difference. The
> memory manager should swap out the unused portion. 

The static array will _use_ as much memory.  It may allocate more, but
if there are 100 entries used in the list then the array will also use
as much - 100 entries.

The swap may be in unused _segments_ or possibly _pages_ and not
bytes. That is the static array will _use_ a number of pages - rounded
up to give more.

> If doesn't, paging can kill
> performance. When running the Demo on a smallish machine (48M) under Win-9x,
> there were 350 page-ins per second, sustained. That's a BAD memory manager, and
> the same one used for the first comparison benchmark.

Of course with Windows LocalAlloc you may be taking a new segment for
each memory block, or you may be using some system heap, or just about
anything - who knows what all the various types of Windows does ?

> Not if they are freed in the reverse of the order they were acquired. The memory
> manager will coalesce the little areas into a single big one. I do that in the
> Demo. A comment reads:
> *> To prevent fragmentation, resources such as memory and files should be
> *> released in the reverse order they were acquired. 

Is that your comment or someone else's ?  What guarantees do you have
that this in fact happens in Windows ?  How can you possibly impose
this as a limited in other than trivial programs ?

> I hate to admit it, but C attracts better programmers than COBOL does.

So what does that say about you working in Cobol ?
```

```
iplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>brk() system call to obtain some large block of memory for the heap.

Who said anything about a heap? All I want is a chunk of memory to call my own
until *I* free it, not some garbage collector or heap manager. Is that asking
too much?

>> I'm assuming both COBOL and C compile to machine language. 
>
>Thay might do, but they might compile to machine code objects that are
>incompatible with some C runtime.  

OO programmers would gag on the phrase "machine code objects". They are
pseudo-code, not "objects". 

>There may be many reasons for this.

There is only one reason: the compiler writer was too lazy or cheap to write a
back-end. He expects users to not notice they're paying every time they execute
a program. 

>> If you're running COBOL under an
>> interpreter, this whole topic is moot because you obviously don't care about
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>waiting for disk transfers.

This shop is runing MF in .int mode, only because that's the default. It runs a
batch job every night which takes two hours and is heavily I-O bound.
Recompiling the code  to .gnt made it run more than twice as fast. Not six times
as fast, as cpu-intensive timing tests report, but still more than double.

>The swap may be in unused _segments_ or possibly _pages_ and not
>bytes. That is the static array will _use_ a number of pages - rounded
>up to give more.

Are you sure ALL operating systems work this way? Now I'm sounding like Mr.
Stevens. I wrote an OS that managed memory to the byte level. Not 2K or 8K, as
advocated by most. I spent a year debating the theoretical advantage with
Computer Scientists before giving up in frustration. 

>Of course with Windows LocalAlloc you may be taking a new segment for
>each memory block, or you may be using some system heap, or just about
>anything - who knows what all the various types of Windows does ?

There's no heap. Just a straightforward memory allocation.

>> Not if they are freed in the reverse of the order they were acquired. The
memory
>> manager will coalesce the little areas into a single big one.

>What guarantees do you have
>that this in fact happens in Windows ?  How can you possibly impose
>this as a limited in other than trivial programs ?

The Demo is not trivial. It solves a real-world problem. 

My 'guarantees' come from timing tests which present real-world evidence of
performance. 

>> I hate to admit it, but C attracts better programmers than COBOL does.
>
>So what does that say about you working in Cobol ?

That the masses let college professors talk them into learning an inferior
language. 

I've known very good programmers who thought the best language was Basic or
assembly language or Smalltalk .. because that's all they knew. I've known bad
programmers who disparaged COBOL and C because programs they write in college
crashed and they couldn't figure out why. Choice of language is 'religious',
made by a decision-process based on emotion rather than reason. 

Robert
```

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

> Who said anything about a heap? All I want is a chunk of memory to call my
> own until *I* free it, not some garbage collector or heap manager. Is that
> asking too much?

malloc() and free() use a heap for several reasons.  Use malloc() to give 
you a chunk of the heap and free() to free it.  Why don't you want them to 
use a heap ?

> There is only one reason: the compiler writer was too lazy or cheap to
> write a back-end. He expects users to not notice they're paying every time
> they execute a program.

>>> I'm assuming both COBOL and C compile to machine language. 
>>
>>Thay might do, but they might compile to machine code objects that are
>>incompatible with some C runtime.  

>OO programmers would gag on the phrase "machine code objects". They are
>pseudo-code, not "objects". 

Geez, do you need every technical term explained to you ?  Are you that 
easily confused ?

The term 'object' has been used to describe blocks of machine code long 
before OO thought of using the term.  This is why output from compilers is 
often *.o or *.OBJ.

>>> I'm assuming both COBOL and C compile to machine language. 
...
> They are pseudo-code, not "objects". 

So, after "assuming they compile to machine code" you now change to having 
be 'pseudo-code' [sic].

>>The swap may be in unused _segments_ or possibly _pages_ and not
>>bytes. That is the static array will _use_ a number of pages - rounded
>>up to give more.
> 
> Are you sure ALL operating systems work this way? 

Study what I said: I used the words _may_ and _possibly_

Unlike you I tend not to use all inclusive hyperbole.  It seems that you 
not only write that way but also read that way regardless of what is 
written.

> Now I'm sounding like Mr. Stevens. I wrote an OS that managed memory 
> to the byte level. Not 2K
> or 8K, as advocated by most. I spent a year debating the theoretical
> advantage with Computer Scientists before giving up in frustration.
```

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

> I didn't misspell  "pseudo-code". Why the [sic]?

Because you probably meant p-code or byte-code, as in MF .int intermediate 
code files.  Pseudocode is quite a different thing:

"Pseudocode

A notation resembling a programming language but not intended
for actual compilation. It usually combines some of the
structure of a programming language with an informal
natural-language description of the computations to be carried
out. It is often produced by CASE systems as a basis for
later hand coding."

>>Perhaps that was because they knew there was no such advantage that you
>>mistakenly claimed.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> character. 

You mean, like you just did to them ?

Perhaps they considered that a cogent rebuttal was just a waste of time, 
perhaps they did give a rebuttal and found that they had wasted their time.

> You got me there. What's the difference between a LocalAlloc and
> HeapAlloc?

They both give a block of memory from a heap, as do a couple of other 
Windows calls: GlobalAlloc, VirtualAlloc.  Perhaps you should read up about 
them all so that you can make an informed choice about these.

> [We could take this thread to alt.flame if it suits you. 

You can post to alt.flame if you prefer, while you post here I will 
continue to point out where you are factually wrong.  Is it that you think 
that 'correction' is the same as 'flame' ?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T12:53:58-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302091253.1b6d4811@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3e45ab96.16845787@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >There is also _no_ guarantee, as you seem to assume, that accessing a 
> >linked list in dynamic memory is in any way faster than array access. It 
> >may be on some systems but not on others.
 
> So run a speed test. Don't make it a 'religious' issue. After you find lists are
> IN FACT faster, what will you do? Change your style?

Choosing my slowest old box so that times may be visible I wrote a
simple program that did :

    100 times:
      allocated 10000 item with a data value set to 1 - 10000
      walked these items 10 times checking data value
      freed the memory

    100 times:
       On a static array OCCURS 10000
       set data value to 1 - 10000
       walked these items 10 times checking data value

Results:

    Dynamic 88 seconds
    Static  27 seconds

    ( I told you it was a .s.l.o.w. machine - a 486 )

Now that I have IN FACT found that dynamic lists are slower, as I
found many years ago in C, will you change your style ?  Or at least
your attitudes ?

With an array it is also possible to access any item at any time just
by setting the index or subscript.  With a linked list with next and
prior pointers the items must be accessed in sequence up to the
position required (unless you store all the pointers in an array).

With an array there is the possibility to do a SEARCH ALL on sorted
data.

Now with an array it may be that, with several data items, the index
may need to be recalculated for accessing each item while with a
dynamic linked list the data items are not subscripted.  This is
easily resolved by simply moving the array item to a work record of
the same layout and accessing the individual data  items without
needing subscripts.  This way the subscript is only calculated once
for the group move.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T18:20:32-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302091820.27611b17@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3e45ab96.16845787@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302091253.1b6d4811@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote

> Now with an array it may be that, with several data items, the index
> may need to be recalculated for accessing each item while with a
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> for the group move.

I have tried this with interesting results.  

I added 20 data items to each list/array group item and set these item
on creation pass and moved them in the 10 traversal paths, using
subsripts for array items and not for the dynamic items.  I also added
a third test where the array item was manipulated in a work area and
group move to/from the array.

The results were somewhat long so I only did 10 cycles rather than 100
of each. The first set of results should be sacled to 9sec (dynamic)
and 3sec(static)

Dynamic was still the slowest at 27sec, Static using index subscripts
was 20 seconds, static using a work area was 22 seconds.

The overhead of dynamic was still around 6 seconds over that of array
index subscripts.  The use of 20 subscripts being faster than similar
moves into a work area plus a group move indicates that someone has
done an excellent job of optimising with Fujitsu.

Obviously a multiply is not done for every subscript usage, 

Other systems may give completely different results.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** Steve Thompson <s_thompson#NO#SPAM_@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T22:11:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3E471848.72BDDD09@ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3e45ab96.16845787@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302091253.1b6d4811@posting.google.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
<snip>
> With an array it is also possible to access any item at any time just
> by setting the index or subscript.  With a linked list with next and
> prior pointers the items must be accessed in sequence up to the
> position required (unless you store all the pointers in an array).
<snip>

Shall we go under the covers for a bit of interesting
system's stuff?

The array, being static and fixed in size (we assume this
here) is an interesting thing in that we have a local of
reference issue. Looking at cache lines and the like, we see
that the pre-fetch logic causes the array elements to
somehow be in the cache when you go to look at the next
element (assuming sequential processing).

However, the index tends to be IN CACHE and STAYS there for
the duration of your time slice while you are manipulating
it to determine the next element you want to handle.

The Linked List on the other hand may not be able to enjoy
the locality of reference, because the item is obtained and
then linked to the next. Where it is in storage is not
DETERMINABLE (unless you know something about that
architecture's memory allocation scheme...). 

This lack of locality of reference may also tend toward
items being paged out and so you incur a paging delay while
the page you need is obtained and then "fixed" in storage.
Now the instruction gets re-driven, which causes the cache
miss to fetch the line(s) into cache...

Hmmmmm. And I'm not talking about a mainframe here, because
this kind of thing happens to all Virtual Storage based
architectures that use cache. I'm not going to address a
modern FAST machines that is 64 bit oriented... 
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** jzitzelb@tsys.com (Joe Zitzelberger)
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T13:59:25-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dcedb8d0.0302101359.71be8b63@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3e45ab96.16845787@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302091253.1b6d4811@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote in message 
<snip>
> Choosing my slowest old box so that times may be visible I wrote a
> simple program that did :
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>     Static  27 seconds

In this specific case you do indeed show that dynamic lists are
slower.  My general experience with Cobol programmers and tables is
that they will allocate one as large as it will ever need to be, then
double it -- just to be sure.

I cannot tell you about how many times I've seen:

  10 ws-so-and-so           occurs 9999 pic x(??).

when the 99.999% case was one or two items in ws-so-and-so.

One very vivid case that I can recall was a 100-byte X 9999-item table
that was mostly used 0 times per account.  However, for each of
millions of accounts it was initialized.

The co-worker that identified that one got major kudos for cutting the
nightly run times in half and saved the company millions in hardware
costs for an almost purchased additional machine.

That is why Cobol programmers should learn to use linked lists.

FWIW, IMNSHO, anyone that accepts money under the guise of being a
programmer ought to understand the concepts of "address" and "memory"
-- something most Cobol programmers seem to feel is unnecesary.  It
isn't cleaver or hard to understand, it is CompSci 101 and a very
simple, easy to understand concept, regardless of language.

<disclaimer>You can certainly abuse linked lists, as in your example. 
If you know you are going to always have 10000 items you should
preallocate and reuse storage.  One of the first questions that should
be asked when designing any data structure should be "Do I know how
many items it will contain in [most][all] cases?" -- The answer should
drive your decisions.</disclaimer>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Speed test

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-11T17:29:58+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e493317.248177438@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b240rv$ku9$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3e45ab96.16845787@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302091253.1b6d4811@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>Choosing my slowest old box so that times may be visible I wrote a
>simple program that did :
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>    Static  27 seconds

I duplicated the test, producing these results:

  Dynamic 1.64
  Static      .75

Ratios are 3.3 (yours) and 2.2 (mine).  

Next I changed the test to work with variable-length strings rather than
integers. The array walker had to compare 30 bytes, whereas the dynamic list
walker had the additional overhead of OCCURS .. DEPENDING but then compared 10
bytes. Results were: 

  Dynamic  4.97
  Static      5.00

I'll retract my claim that lists are faster. Speed is the same for
variable-length strings. 

Test programs were compiled with Micro Focus, run on a 450 MHz Sun Solaris.
Source program follows.

 identification division.
 program-id. testmem2.
*author. Robert Wagner.
*  Testing speed of array vs. list.
*  Finding:
*   number           array  .75
*                    list  1.64
*   fixed len string array 4.97
*                    list  1.80
*   variable string  array 4.97
*                    list  5.00
 data division.
 working-storage section.
 01  unqualified-variables.
     05  n                         comp-5  pic s9(09).
     05  mem-pointer                       pointer.
     05  mem-size comp-5 pic s9(09) value 14.
     05  flags    comp-5 pic s9(09) value zero.
     05  string-length comp-5 pic s9(09) value 10.
     05  first-list-entry                  pointer.
     05  start-time.
         10  start-hours                   pic  9(02).
         10  start-minutes                 pic  9(02).
         10  start-seconds                 pic  9(02).
         10  start-hundredths              pic  9(02).
     05  end-time.
         10  end-hours                     pic  9(02).
         10  end-minutes                   pic  9(02).
         10  end-seconds                   pic  9(02).
         10  end-hundredths                pic  9(02).
     05  elapsed-time                      pic  z(04).99.

 01  array-structure.
     05  a-string occurs 20000             pic x(30).
 linkage section.
 01  list-entry.
     05  next-entry                        pointer.
     05  l-string.
         10  filler occurs 1 to 30 depending on string-length pic x.

 procedure division.
 main.
*  construct array
     display 'array ' with no advancing
     accept start-time from time
     perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > 10000
         move 'test text' to a-string (n)
     end-perform
*  access array
     perform 1000 times
         perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > 10000
             if  a-string (n) not equal to 'test text'
                 display 'test failed' goback
             end-if
         end-perform
     end-perform
     perform display-time
*  construct list
     display 'list  ' with no advancing
     accept start-time from time
     perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > 10000
         call 'CBL_ALLOC_MEM' using mem-pointer, by value mem-size, flags
         if  n equal to 1
             set first-list-entry to mem-pointer
         else
             set next-entry to mem-pointer
         end-if
         set address of list-entry to mem-pointer
         move 'test text'  to l-string
         set next-entry to null
     end-perform
     perform 1000 times
         set address of list-entry to first-list-entry
         perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > 10000
             if  l-string not equal to 'test text'
                 display 'test failed' goback
             end-if
             set address of list-entry to next-entry
         end-perform
     end-perform
     perform display-time

     goback.

 display-time.
     accept end-time from time
     compute elapsed-time rounded =
        (((((((end-hours * 60) +
           end-minutes) * 60) +
           end-seconds) * 100) +
           end-hundredths) -
        ((((((start-hours * 60) +
           start-minutes) * 60) +
           start-seconds) * 100) +
           start-hundredths)) / 100
     display  elapsed-time ' secs'.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T09:11:29-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b28mg1$18gc$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net...
> >If an operation is successful this is indicated in the _first_byte_ of
> >the file status.  The second byte may contain additional information.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

ANSI X3.23-1985 allows for 04 (wrong-length record), 05 (absent optional
file) and 07 (CLOSE with NO REWIND or other reel-oriented action on a
non-reel/unit medium).   ISO/IEC 1989:2002 allows for 02 (duplicate key
encountered on a READ NEXT or READ PREVIOUS, or duplicate key created on a
WRITE or REWRITE) in addition.

> >> The most common codes are 95 file not found on open,
> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Actually, there is no Standard for file not found.

39 is defined as ""The OPEN statement is unsuccessful because a conflict has
been detected between the fixed file attributes and the attributes specified
for that file in the source unit."  I can see that an implementor might
decide that "file not found" could be interpreted as qualifying.

>
> >> 10 end of file (not an error) and
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> Read produces 23; start/read next does not. I didn't show read in chapter
8.
>

Really?  I thought START produced a 23 on an absent optional file.  Common
it might not be, but impossible, no.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote:

>
>On 10-Feb-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
>
>> >Typically it was used for applications such as data entry, where you read

>> >first record to find out where to place your entry record, update the first
>> >record and then jump directly to the entry record.
>>
>> The original poster, Richard, said "Relative files are very fast ..". We
don't
>> need high speed in data entry, we're running at human speed. And all you're
>> doing is appending a record to the end of file. OPEN EXTEND of a sequential
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>usage of relative files was about 20 years ago).

The same thing that happens with a relative file -- you get 'file locked' return
code, wait a random time (a millisecond or two), and try again. There are two
better solutions: 

..Create a separate file for each data entry session (there can be more than one
per machine). Collect them into a single file at end of day.

..Make the file writer a separate task, which will single-thread your writes and
avoid all those opens and closes. 

Robert
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T23:27:39-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302102327.5bd87f88@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b28pub$eh8$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3e47fab0.168189712@news.optonline.net> <b28vv3$h2r$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3e480db0.173054456@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >What happens when 100 users are writing a new record in an OPEN EXTEND?  (My
> >usage of relative files was about 20 years ago).
> 
> The same thing that happens with a relative file -- you get 'file locked' return
> code, wait a random time (a millisecond or two), and try again.

While you might OPEN a RELATIVE file EXTEND, you can only do so in
SEQUENTIAL access mode - which defeats the whole point of it being
RELATIVE.

OPENing a RELATIVE file I-O in RANDOM mode allows many users to share
the file with record locking (on system that support this) which makes
much more sense. A control record (number 1, say) is used to obtain
the 'next record' number and this is read with lock and rewritten as
required to obtain another number.

> There are two better solutions: 

Once you understand how RELATIVE files are _actually_ used then there
is no need for a 'better' system.
 
> ..Create a separate file for each data entry session (there can be more than one
> per machine). Collect them into a single file at end of day.

This may, or may not, be better. What advantages would you attribute
to this ?
 
> ..Make the file writer a separate task, which will single-thread your writes and
> avoid all those opens and closes. 

What 'opem and closes' ?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b28pub$eh8$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3e47fab0.168189712@news.optonline.net> <b28vv3$h2r$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3e480db0.173054456@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302102327.5bd87f88@posting.google.com>`

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>> ..Create a separate file for each data entry session (there can be more than
one
>> per machine). Collect them into a single file at end of day.
>
>This may, or may not, be better. What advantages would you attribute
>to this ?

You'll never get a 'file locked' or 'record 1 locked'.

>> ..Make the file writer a separate task, which will single-thread your writes
and
>> avoid all those opens and closes. 
>
>What 'open and closes' ?

You need some way to flush disk buffers, to avoid losing data when the machine
crashes mid-day. If the file system does not offer COMMIT, the alternative is
closing the file frequently.

Robert
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-02-13T21:12:44+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b2fk53$19g$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3e480db0.173054456@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302102327.5bd87f88@posting.google.com> <3e4b4202.544695@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

>>> ..Create a separate file for each data entry session (there can be more
>>> than
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> You'll never get a 'file locked' or 'record 1 locked'.

Unless someone left their machine with the program running and the file 
open, then when the collection program is trying the read the data, the 
file will be locked until someone closes it down, delaying the collection 
process.

With a shared file at least the collection program can read and mark (or 
delete) all the completed records without worrying if the stations are 
still open.
 
>>> ..Make the file writer a separate task, which will single-thread your
>>> writes
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> alternative is closing the file frequently.

If the open and closes are needed then in what way does having a file 
writer as a separate task "avoid all those opens and closes" ?

In fact, with a shared file under MF there is usually no need to close the 
file and reopen it as flushing is automatic, it being required to ensure 
data integrity. It is only when the files are exclusive that it holds onto 
buffers and then data is threatened by power cuts, such as when each 
station has its own file, or when there is a single separate task as file 
writer with an exclusive file.

Interestingly, you chose the exact two mechanisms that can lose data as the 
the ones you think of as 'better'.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T17:05:35-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302101705.59960c80@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0302072309.1c92180b@posting.google.com> <3e4565df.254562516@news.optonline.net> <b28pub$eh8$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3e47fab0.168189712@news.optonline.net> <b28vv3$h2r$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard.brazee@cusys.edu> wrote 

> > >Typically it was used for applications such as data entry, where you read the
> > >first record to find out where to place your entry record, update the first
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> usage of relative files was about 20 years ago).

They don't.  OPEN EXTEND is an exclusive operation.  If a second user
tries to do this the open will fail with a '9x' file status indicating
file locked. The program should delay, or at least dispatch (release
time slice) and retry. The file should be closed as soon as possible.

OPEN EXTEND is not appropriate to RELATIVE files, only SEQUENTIAL. 
With RELATIVE files the first (control) record is locked while being
updated to ensure that the same relative number is not used twice.
```

```
<snip>

I read down to where you were discussing the different
"margins" used in COBOL.

I'd like to point out my objections to the free format COBOL
which the IBM reps to J4 discarded.
How do you provide a COBOL based software product which a
client has source for, but which you also provide updates?
Specifically, how do you merge your updates into the updates
the client has put on your software? Since free format gets
rid of the initial "COBOL" sequence numbers, you have no way
to merge the "delta decks" together. [DUMB idea IBM J4
reps!]

You also mentioned that there are 4 DIVISIONs in COBOL but
only 2 are now used, and the IDENTIFICATION DIVISION is
nothing but comments anyway. Hmmmmm. And just what is the
PROGRAM-ID? What happens when you don't specify it, or you
leave it the same from source to source?

ENVIRONMENT is used for more than SELECT/ASSIGN statements.
Particularly in LARGE COMPLEX COBOL programs.

DATA is also used. Without it, how would the PROCEDURE
DIVISION be functional? Or did you mis-type when you said
that only 2 divisions are needed (As I recall, with the
compilers that I use, you don't put in the ID Division and
you don't compile)?

OO COBOL is not really used. Hmmmmm. I guess the system that
I wrote for an NT environment is only vaporware? The
accounting system I'm preparing to work on will also be
vaporware? Perhaps I'm ahead of my time. And maybe there are
problems with CLASS libraries, but then I tend to develop
all of my own libraries when I'm building a marketable
product (so I can control what is happening to me, and not
be subject to the whim of others).

Well, you wanted critical remarks. Perhaps you need a bit
more experience with COBOL before you come off with the idea
that you are an expert and know all concerning COBOL (which
another poster showed you did not - specifically Mr. Klein).
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T05:29:50+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01c2cffc$382a4e00$b491f243@chottel>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com>`

```
I like to start all of my paragraph names with fixed length number starting
at a fixed column, usually column 8.  For example "10000-paraname".  That
way I can use the ROSCOE command
"deletex 13 13 /-/" to get all the paragraph names. I save them in a ROSCOE
member named T1.  Then with "deletex /go to/", "deletex /perform/",
"deletex /call/", ...etc with each result saved in a separate ROSCOE member
T2, T3,  etc. Then a "merge T1 T2 T3 ..." will give me all the info I need
to construct a structure chart.  In fact it is quite easy to write a
general RPF which is passed the program member name and does all of this
automatically.

I also find that mixing uppercase and lowercase complicates searching.  For
example I want to search for either "spaces" or "SPACES" not "Spaces" or
"sPaces" etc, and I don't want to have to bother with converting to all
uppercase or all lowercase before searching.

For keeping track of program changes I do the following.  The orginal
program contains boilerplate documentation which contains  slighty
different info for either online programs or batch programs.  At the end of
this is the start of a modification log. The original program usually has
columns 1 to 6 blank except for LIBRARIAN -INC statments.  For the first
modification I will put "H01" in columns 1 to 3 in the mod log
documentation and on all lines inserted.  For deleted lines, sometimes I
delete them ,and sometimes I comment them out with "H01****". It is a
judgement call depending upon whether I think the change may be taken out
later and revert back to yhe original code.

The ROSCOE line command "NC" for "note cobol" will create flower boxes for
you.

Here is an example:

      ****************************************************************     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *                      FP7030                   03/12/91       *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    FPC TO FFC CONVERSION PROGRAM.                            *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      ****************************************************************     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    SUPPORTING INFORMATION:                                   *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - CLIENT-------------:  xxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxxx*     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - CLIENT CONTACT-----:  yyyyyyyyy (301-nnn-nnnn)        *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - PROGRAMMER---------:  CHARLIE HOTTEL (302-nnn-nnnn)   *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - PROGRAM------------:  FP703001                        *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - MAP----------------:  N/A                             *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - FUNCTION CODE------:  N/A                             *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - PROCESSING MODE----:  BATCH                           *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *      - PURPOSE------------:                                  *     
    
      *         THIS PROGRAM CREATES THE NEW FFC DATABASE, FOR THE   *     
    
      *         FP&F REDSIGN, FROM THE EXSISTING FPC DATABASE.       *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    GENERAL OUTLINE--------:                                  *     
    
      *     1. ACCEPT PARAMETER INDICATING DISTRICT TO BE CONVERTED. *     
    
      *        "00" MEANS CONVERT ALL DISTRICTS.                     *     
    
      *     2. DELETE ANY EXSISTING FFC RECORDS.                     *     
    
      *     3. READ FPC DATABASE BY FPCK4:                           *     
    
      *          VIOL-CODE/VIOL-DIST/VIOL-TYPE.                      *     
    
      *     4. BUILD FFC RECORD FROM FIELDS IN FFC RECORD.           *     
    
      *     5. CALCULATE OPEN CASE COUNTS, FIRST AND LAST VIOLATION  *     
    
      *        DATES FOR LIQUIDATED DAMAGES, SEIZURES, AND PENALTIES.*     
    
      *     6. ADD NEW FFC RECORDS AND DISPLAY DUPLICATE FFC RECORDS.*     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    PROCESS DESCRIPTION----:                                  *     
    
      *     1. ACCEPT PARAMETER INDICATING DISTRICT TO BE CONVERTED. *     
    
      *        "00" MEANS CONVERT ALL DISTRICTS.                     *     
    
      *     2. DELETE ANY EXSISTING FFC RECORDS.                     *     
    
      *     3. READ FPC DATABASE BY FPCK4:                           *     
    
      *          VIOL-CODE/VIOL-DIST/VIOL-TYPE.                      *     
    
      *     4. BUILD FFC RECORD FROM FIELDS IN FFC RECORD.           *     
    
      *          ADD CENTURY TO DATES.                               *     
    
      *     5. CALCULATE OPEN CASE COUNTS, FIRST AND LAST VIOLATION  *     
    
      *        DATES FOR LIQUIDATED DAMAGES, SEIZURES, AND PENALTIES.*     
    
      *         -THESE VALUES ARE CALCULATED BECAUSE THE VALUES IN   *     
    
      *          THE FPC RECORDS ARE CONSIDERED UNRELIABLE.          *     
    
      *         -READ ALL FPV RECORDS FOR VIOLATOR IN THIS PORT USING*     
    
      *          FPVK6, VIOL-CODE/VIOL-DIST/VIOL-TYPE/CASE-NUMBER    *     
    
      *          -EACH TIME THE CASE NUMBER CHANGES                  *     
    
      *           -READ FPV USING FPVK1:                             *     
    
      *            CASE-NUMBER/VIOLATOR-SEQ/VIOLATION-SEQ            *     
    
      *            WITH THE SEQUENCE NUMBERS SET TO "01" TO DETERMINE*     
    
      *            THE PRIMARY VIOLATION TYPE.                       *     
    
      *           -READ FPE USING FPEK1, VIOLATION                   *     
    
      *              THIS IS USED TO DETERMINE VIOLATION TYPE              
    
      *           -READ FPA USING FPAK1, CASE-NUMBER                 *     
    
      *              THIS IS USED TO DETERMINE CURRENT STATUS              
    
      *           -READ FPF USING FPFK1, STATUS-CODE                 *     
    
      *              THIS IS USED TO DETERMINE OPEN/COLSED STATUS          
    
      *           _CALCULATE DATES AND COUNTS                        *     
    
      *     6. ADD NEW FFC RECORDS AND DISPLAY DUPLICATE FFC RECORDS.*     
    
      *        -THE MASTER KEY FOR FFC IS                            *     
    
      *         VIOLTR-ID-NBR/VIOLTR-DIST-ID. SINCE THIS COMBINATION *     
    
      *         IS NOT UNIQUE ON FPC DUPLICATES ARE POSSIBLE.        *     
    
      *        -IF THE FFC RECORD BUILT IS A DUPLICATE               *     
    
      *           DISPLAY ERROR MESSAGE AND BYPASS                   *     
    
      *         ELSE                                                 *     
    
      *           ADD NEW FFC RECORD.                                *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    INCLUDES---------------:  LCS001C  - LIBCTL WORK FIELDS   *     
    
      *                              DDFFC01  - FFC RECORD LAYOUT    *     
    
      *                              DDFPA01  - FPA RECORD LAYOUT    *     
    
      *                              DDFPC01  - FPC RECORD LAYOUT    *     
    
      *                              DDFPE01  - FPE RECORD LAYOUT    *     
    
      *                              DDFPF01  - FPF RECORD LAYOUT    *     
    
      *                              DDFPV01  - FPV RECORD LAYOUT    *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    SUBROUTINES------------:  NONE                            *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    FILE TABLES------------:  FFC - FP&F VIOLATOR HISTORY- I/O*     
    
      *                              FPA - FP&F CASE            - I  *     
    
      *                              FPC - FP&F VIOLATOR        - I  *     
    
      *                              FPE - FP&F VIOLATION       - I  *     
    
      *                              FPF - FP&F STATUS CODE     - I  *     
    
      *                              FPV - FP&F CASE VIOLATOR AND    *     
    
      *                                    VIOLATION ASSOCIATION- I  *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    PFKEYS DEFINED---------:  N/A                             *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    MASK LAYOUT------------:  N/A                             *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    FREQUENCY--------------:  ONE TIME CONVERSION PROGRAM     *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    DISTRIBUTION-----------:  N/A                             *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    EXECUTION JCL----------:  N/A                             *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    REPORT LAYOUTS---------:  None                            *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      *    OPERATIONAL CONCERNS---:  None                            *     
    
      *                                                              *     
    
      ****************************************************************     
    
      *             MODIFICATION LOG                                 *
      ****************************************************************     
    
H01   *  C-nnnn     CHANGE TITLE      CHARLIE HOTTEL  ccyy/mm/dd     *
H01   *                                                              *     
    
H01   *  DESCRIPTION OF THE CHANGE                                   *     
    
      ****************************************************************
```

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)

- **Message-ID:** `<3e463c4d.53897502@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com> <01c2cffc$382a4e00$b491f243@chottel>`

```
Naming paragraphs "10000-paraname" originated in the days when we worked with
printed listings. The number provided an 'index' to help us find the paragraph
in large monolithic programs. Now that we have text editors with search
capability, there is no longer a reason to index paragraph names in that way.

Assuming you have Version Control software (you mentioned Librarian), a
"modification log" in the source code is obsolete. 

What is GO TO? A program doesn't go anywhere. It sits in memory processing
transactions. 

Robert

"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote:

>I like to start all of my paragraph names with fixed length number starting
>at a fixed column, usually column 8.  For example "10000-paraname".  That
…[251 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
than 1,000 COBOL programs in the first year. Whereas most Programming Standards
prohibit changes outside the scope of the assignment, mine encouraged changing
anything if doing so made the code clearer, easier to understand. We had synergy
going ... the code base was better than any individual, including myself, could
have written on his own.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T13:15:58-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b28tq5$c7e$1@slb5.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com> <01c2cffc$382a4e00$b491f243@chottel> <3e463c4d.53897502@news.optonline.net> <01c2d05f$be9fa800$e7caf943@chottel> <3e46e109.96076606@news.optonline.net> <b28q67$em9$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3e47f4a1.166638930@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert, (and anyone else "discussing" what is and is not "standard" - aka
ANSI/ISO conforming - versus "common among the compilers that I am familiar
with" - may I suggest that you run (occasionally) a compile with your
vendor's "standard flagging" mechanism - which usually (not always) is
DIFFERENT from its "language dialect selection" mechanism.

For example -
   with a relatively current IBM compiler
        CMPR2 is '74 Standard language selection
               vs
        NOCMPR2 is '85 Standard language selection

while
         NOFLASTD   doesn't ask for Standard conformance checking
                vs
          FLAGSTD(H,O) selects conformance and obsolete flagging

    ***

Similarly with Fujitsu's compiler
           LANGLVL(85) selects '85 Standard language support
                vs
          LANGLVL(74) select '74 Standard language support

while
        NOFLAGSW doesn't ask for Standard conformance checking
            vs
        FLAGSW(STH)  request high-level conformance checking

***

The important thing to remember is that many (most? all?) compilers allow
for MANY extensions - even when an ANSI/ISO conformance "language dialect"
is selected.  It is only when "conformance checking" is turned on, that all
ANSI/ISO conforming compilers tell you which syntax is truly "Standard" and
which is not. In other words, the only SOURCE CODE that is "guaranteed to be
portable between standard conforming compilers" is source code that gets
ZERO flagging messages when standard conforming flagging is turned on.
OBVIOUSLY,  this is "source code" flagging only - and won't tell you if your
compiler actually produces "conforming" object code  (many compilers have
"extension" or "non-conforming" run-time options that may or may not be the
default  cf.  IBM's NOCBLQDA).

NOTE:
   With the 2002 Standard, there are additional "flagging" mechanism to
highlight various types of syntax that are "conforming" may be less
portable.  I won't go into them here - until some compiler vendor actually
provides such support.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

>NOTE:
>   With the 2002 Standard, there are additional "flagging" mechanism to
>highlight various types of syntax that are "conforming" may be less
>portable.  I won't go into them here - until some compiler vendor actually
>provides such support.

I wish there was a feature allowing the shop to disallow older features kept in
the standard for backward compatibility, and vendor extensions the shop wants to
disallow without losing the whole dialect. Most shops have a Programming
Standards document, but no way to enforce it mechanically.

I wrote a pre-compiler to do this. It also does some beautification such as
indentation and lining up pictures. In addition, it gives the program a quality
grade which it inserts as a comment. The last is a good way to improve code
quality without lifting a finger. Programmers shoot for 100 out of pride, which
is more positive than being rapped on the knuckles for bad practices. 

Robert
```

```
Steve,
  Regarding "how to distribute source code changes - in a free form
reference format environent" - my suggestion would by using the

   *>

in-line comment factility, e.g.  (2002 conforming source code)

*> Notice there is no Identification Division header       00100
 Program-Id.
*> 00200
   MyPGM.
*> 00300
Procedure Division.                                                    *>
00400
   Dispaly "an updated line"                                        *> 00450
added by fix 1234
   GoBack
*> 00500
     .
*> 00600


(sorry if proportional font "messes" this up)

etc - assuming they get updated for 2002 Standard source code.  This, of
course, assumes that IBM - your environment, I believe - ever produces a
2002 Standard compiler - which they have so far given ZERO indication of)
```

```
My TARGET environment may be NT, it may be z/OS (mainframe).
It may also be Linux (mainframe or PC based). My ORIGINAL
source environment is always other than a mainframe. So the
idea of the comments doesn't work very well.

In any environment where I am, I can always write the
equivalent of IEBUPDTE, so even if it (or equivalent
function) doesn't exist, I can provide it.

This, BTW, is one of the draw backs of C/C++ (among others).
They have made no provision for client modifying source for
client specific requirements. It has been assumed that one
is to do OCO product delivery (if not explicitly assumed, it
has been by default of the design).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Steve Thompson <s_thompson#NO#SPAM_@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T21:57:42-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3E471526.F4526718@ix.netcom.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com> <b24poo$jcq$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
"William M. Klein" wrote:
<snip>

I got to thinking about this some more. I don't think you
and some others get the point.

If I develop an accounting system and provide it both as
object and as source, you have the ability to run it as
shipped, or to add/modify functions/features and/or business
rules.

However, if you also expect to get updates to the code that
are caused by certain changes to accounting
standards/practices, you will need a way for me to provide
that update to you so that you can apply it.

Now, if you are using Librarian, and I'm using ChangeMan,
how do you apply my changes to the source that you've
modified with the updates I'm sending you?

So, getting rid of the COBOL sequence numbers removes the
ability for me to publish fixes/changes to the source that I
provide, unless I replace it all. Then how do you merge your
fixes into the new code if I can scramble it all around,
since after all this is a "new release"?

This problem will manifest itself regardless of the platform
that I start with and the one that the customers use because
they may not be the same with the same software control
products.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T21:09:47-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b2755t$t27$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com> <b24poo$jcq$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3E471526.F4526718@ix.netcom.com>`

```
Steve,
  Are you aware that "fixed form reference format" *is* maintained in the
2002 COBOL Standard (and is NOT in the "archaic" much less "obsolete"
category).

Therefore, if you CREATE the original source code - there is NOTHING to say
you can't/shouldn't use "traditional" fixed form reference format (with
columns 1-6 used for whatever you want - and anything you think appropriate
between the R-margin and the end of the "physical" line of code).

Therefore, you can continue to do whatever you want.  (Of course even in the
'85 Standard, columns 1-6 are *NOT* required to include numeric values -
much less numeric values in sequential order).

It is true that when/if you "distribute" fixed form reference format source
code that your customers can "modify" or "add to" - that they can ADD free
form reference format in the "middle" of your source code - but that would
require a specific directive (on a line by itself) to switch to/from such
code.

   ****

Having said all of the above, I will say that I *personally* don't
like/trust software that is distributed in "source code".  Having worked for
a couple of mainframe and pc software vendors, I *strongly* prefer providing
"object code only" with well-documented "user exits" than allowing customers
to "modify" the applications that we distribute.  However, I do understand
that other vendors have different business requirements that MAY require
modifiable source code.
```

```
> Steve,

> 2002 COBOL Standard (and is NOT in the "archaic" much less "obsolete"

<snip>

Yes, I am aware. However, the "free form" makes much sense.
I have for a long time wished that I could use VARIABLE
length statements to COBOL, FORTRAN, ALC and others.

Variable length statements allow one to put a complete
literal on *A* line and not have to split it across n lines.

Also, certain logic is better written in a line rather than
being split across n lines.

And I was thinking about CPCS/CPCS-II, along with ASI's
accounting software (which a client of mine just got rid
of).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T05:21:57+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e4736ec.118066241@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com> <b24poo$jcq$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net> <3E471526.F4526718@ix.netcom.com>`

```
>So, getting rid of the COBOL sequence numbers removes the
>ability for me to publish fixes/changes to the source that I
>provide, unless I replace it all. Then how do you merge your
>fixes into the new code if I can scramble it all around,
>since after all this is a "new release"?

Industry-standard practice is replace the whole source module, let downstream
users deal with merging changes. I've encountered it in many shops. It's not
pretty, but that's life in the real world. 

At the user level, they run a diff between your new code and the old. User
enhancements show up clearly unless you've restructured your program. I've never
seen that happen.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-02-09T20:02:04+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b24ufn$530$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <3E45D8FE.F38DDB9C@ix.netcom.com>`

```
Steve Thompson wrote:

> How do you provide a COBOL based software product which a
> client has source for, but which you also provide updates?
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> reps!]

You use a Web based source code control system.

> You also mentioned that there are 4 DIVISIONs in COBOL but
> only 2 are now used, and the IDENTIFICATION DIVISION is
> nothing but comments anyway. Hmmmmm. And just what is the
> PROGRAM-ID? What happens when you don't specify it, or you
> leave it the same from source to source?

On some systems the Program-Id is used as the module name in the CANCEL. 
That is the CALL is to the file name (.EXE, .int, etc) and the CANCEL is of 
the Program-Id.  If they don't match then the CANCEL fails.

In other cases the Program-Id is required, such as in nested programs.
```

```
Note to all:  These comments are my own and are not reflective of Sams
Computer Publishing, Flexus or Fujitsu Software Corporation (all of
whom I am associated in one way or another)

robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote in message news:<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net>...

Two comments here - the full price of the book is $24.99.  (USD). 
It's generally available on Amazon for < $20.00 and used copies as low
as $9.99.

It is also available via NetLibrary, but I don't know the price.

The common ground viewer led a deservedly so short life.  It's not
that the viewer is corrupt - but it doesn't install cleanly on
versions of Windows that were released after the book and Version 3 of
the Fujitsu compiler was released.  With the Windows compatibility
toolkit it does install under Windows 2000 - I have not tried under
XP, but I have readers who are using the compiler at least under XP
after setting Windows 98 compatibility for each EXE involved.

> 
> Fujitsu
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

The pricing cited here is obsolete.  See http://www.netcobol.com for
current pricing.  One may no longer purchase just the "Standard"
edition, so the current price is higher.

> Liant RM
> 
> Produces interpreted p-code, which means slow execution. Has not been
> enhanced in ten years.

This is a bit misleading.  Liant in the last year has released version

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

> generated much discussion but little usable code. The problem with OO
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> nor of OO. Its grammar was poorly designed.

Not to Quibble, but I don't really think this is true.  In actuality
LOC goes down with OO COBOL implementation because inline method
invocation is very terse.  It's a silly argument at best and a
religious one at worse.

As far as design of Grammar - I'd be interested in a separate thread
discussing what you find poorly designed about it.

> 
> Numbers:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

This is a compiler/vendor specific extension and is not implemented
the same way by all PC COBOL Vendors.

> 
> Although a 16-bit word can hold values to 32,767, your Cobol compiler
> may not allow values greater than 9,999 because pic 9(04) means four
> decimal digits.

That said - even the Fujitsu compiler allows you to COMPUTE the larger
value into the comp-5 (or similarly defined) field when you set the
NOTRUNC compiler option.  Others I have worked with behave simiarly -
these include Realia, RM, MicroFocus and AcuCOBOL.

> There is usually no reason to give the indicator a name, and doing so
> can make your code confusing. For example, moving 'e' to
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> false value.

Setting an indicator to FALSE is supported in the 2002 standard and
some other compilers have implemented this feature.

> 
> Floating-point:
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

This is a compiler extension.  For example, one compiler uses COMP-1
to mean the same thing that others use COMP-5 for.

> 
> set a-pointer to address of a-structure
> set address of a-structure to a-pointer
> set an-indicator to true
> set an-index to 1

Set ... to Address is a vendor extension.  For example, with Fujitsu
instead you use an Intrinsic Function (Also an extension).

> 
> * Learn to type. The leading cause of bad programming is fear of
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> understand. Chapter 5 will teach you logic.

I just have to comment here.  I tell anyone who listens the two most
important classes I ever took were:

Typing 
Symbolic Logic

This is EXCELLENT ADVICE!

> structures, if, evacuate, and perform.

I thing this is "Evaluate"

> 
> If an operation fails, file-status will be non-zero. The most common
> codes are 95 file not found on open, 10 end of file (not an error) and
> 22 insert failed because record already exists. You should test this
> after every file operation.

The 95 here should be 35.

> 
> Fujitsu uses Btrieve for its indexed file system. The other compilers
> use proprietary formats. Access is amazingly fast with all.

This is not true.  Fujitsu has the OPTION to use BTRIEVE files,
Fujitsu native indexed files are something else.

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> end-search


Note: While most implementations I am aware of implement Search All as
a binary search, it is not a requirement that they do so.
```

```
industry-standard syntax i.e. same as Micro Focus and IBM, as shown above. You
can see it in my demo, which compiles under Fujitsu v3 and runs correctly.

>> codes are 95 file not found on open, 10 end of file (not an error) and

Fujitsu               35
Micro Focus       9-013
RM                    9-013

Realia                92

>Fujitsu native indexed files are something else.

Robert
```

```
this DOES include Micro Focus, IBM - workstation and mainframe, Fujitsu,

*ALL* issue a "35" (in a PIC XX files status code field) on a "file not

all I don't personally know of any that do)

this - and it will surprise me GREATLY.
```

```
file status is expected)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-02-11T11:08:15-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0302111108.6a921540@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <bfdfc3e8.0302092127.590f07ec@posting.google.com> <3e47d82f.159355696@news.optonline.net> <bfdfc3e8.0302101555.38c8000e@posting.google.com> <3e484161.186289388@news.optonline.net> <b29j9e$qn8$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3e485f5e.193967854@news.optonline.net> <b29t75$nfh$1@slb0.atl.mindspring.net> <3e48eb07.229726521@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> This is strange. Given that compilers return 35 when the "conforming" option is
> on, why not use that value always? I see no advantage for IBM, as an example, to
> return 90 when the option is off. The only possible explanation is backward
> compatibility with pre-85 versions. 
 
It isn't strange at all.  When a site implemented a new '85 compiler
to replace its previous '74 compiler they wanted to compile all their
existing programs with the new compiler so they could use the new
run-time.  These programs checked for the pre-'85 file status codes so
would be compiled with NOANS85 or similar.

As programs were amended to use '85 features they could use the
ANS85"SYNTAX" so the file-status checking did not need updating, but
could be to use either.

New programs and converted old programs could use ANS85.

Doing this spreads the conversion workload over time while keeping the
whole system working.  What would be your solution ?  Rewriting the
lot in a week or two ?

The advantage to the compiler vendor (such as IBM) is that this code
change is not a barrier to adopting the new compiler in a site and it
can completely replace the old one with no disruption.

The advantage to the site is that they don't need to waste time
rewiting old code and can start to use new features.

Your site seems to be still in the 'thinking about change' state from
the late 80s that use a default of ANS85"SYNTAX", now _that_ is
strange.

How hard is this ?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** bsulliv@legacyj.com (Brian Sullivan)
- **Date:** 2003-02-18T13:55:03-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f8041505.0302181355.e9ab3f0@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <bfdfc3e8.0302092127.590f07ec@posting.google.com> <3e47d82f.159355696@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote in message news:<3e47d82f.159355696@news.optonline.net>...
> >> LegacyJ PERCobol
> >> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> That seems to dissipated. Nowadays it's interpreted. 

Hello,

Just a quick note here.  All commercial Javas (with the exception of a
few traditional ahead-of-time compilers) use JIT technology; it's
never interpreted when executed in real code.  They don't stress JITs
now because the implementations are now a layer above that in design.

Sun's HotSpot is a layering on top of JIT, determining dynamically
where and how to optimize and JIT the code.  The very first run over
code determines how it's used; it tracks the code usage, determining
what is and is not important to optimize.  (For example,
initialization code is barely optimized, while a tight loop is highly
optimized.)  Being dynamically compiled, the speed of the program
actually increases over a run.  HotSpot is different from a JIT in
that a JIT may only optimize behavior that it is 'sure' of, whereas
HotSpot can back out of optimizations and re-optimize according to new
behavior.  Sun and HP use HotSpot.

IBM's primary solution (AIX, OS/390, OS/2, Linux, Windows) is similar,
also layered on a JIT, having different performance strengths from
Sun's implementation.  IBM's is clearly a JIT at its core, shown from
the version screen as (JIT enabled: jitc).  However, its 'mixed-mode'
capabilities also allow a faster startup time and better long-term
performance than a plain JIT.

IBM's AS/400 solution is particularly interesting.  Its Java for
AS/400 precompiles the Java to the actual machine architecture rather
than the virtual architecture on which the other languages for the
platform execute, IBM having deemed Java sufficiently safe to execute
at the lower level.

Because each vendor is free to implement the architecture in any
compatible manner, some are stronger at CPU, others at I/O, some for
servers, some for clients.  BEA's JRocket, for instance, would never
be used for a client, but for long-running servers it's great.

Regards,

Brian Sullivan
LegacyJ Corp.
```


#### ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org>
- **Date:** 2003-02-10T22:45:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<01c2d156$08ec6d20$89ccf943@chottel>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net>`

```
My feedback comes mainly from the IBM mainframe COBOL perspective and I
will use "MC" as an abbreviation for "mainframe COBOL".  Your frequent
references to ASCII lead me to believe you are writing mainly from the PC
perspective so my comments may or may not be relevant to your purpose and
some comments may relate to IBM extensions of no particular interest  to
you. I have tried not to repeat what others have previously said. 

Feedback to Part 1.

You have the comment "*>local variables" next to working-storage section. 
Most of the literature on COBOL faults it for having global variables.  MC
also has a local-storage section.

This may make your usage of local confusing to some people. I think I see
where you are coming from. W-S is global to all performed paragraphs but
you perfer to use call instead and if you do then w-s seems local to you.
But there are at least two other contexts in MC where w-s is global.  In a
recursive program there is only one w-s no matter how many recursive calls.
 Also MC now supports multi-threading and all threads share one w-s with
each thread having it's own l-s.

Under pointers, data field: a-pointer is missing the usage is pointer. On
MC there are three flavors of pointer: pointer, procedure-pointer and
function-pointer.  procedure-pointer is preferred for COBOL-to-COBOL calls
and for calls to Language Environment callable services.  function-pointers
are used to communicate with c/c++ or java native interface.

On MC comp-1 and comp-2 are considered internal floating point.  For
completeness there is also an external floating point.

On transforming a conditional to it's negative you might mention that you
are using DeMorgans Laws. Starting at this point some of your ifs are
missing end-if.

You example of calling a local function was confusing to me. First you say
global defeats the purpose but then your example uses global and on the
very parameter that is being passed. You might want to explain the
difference between call by reference and call by value at this point.


Feedback to Part 2.

Change "files thet open for update but never update" to "files that are
opened for update but are never updated".  You might want to run spell
check.

Your demo program does not always follow your best practices.  The 05 level
above your 88 levels in the first structure has a name, but in part 1 you
advised against this. You also perform paragraphs but in part one you
called this weak.

Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net> wrote in article
<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net>...
> Best Practices - Cobol
> Robert Wagner
> [saved as plain text]
> 

<snip>
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-02-11T04:06:05+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3e4876ad.199935517@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <01c2d156$08ec6d20$89ccf943@chottel>`

```
"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote:

>My feedback comes mainly from the IBM mainframe COBOL perspective and I
>will use "MC" as an abbreviation for "mainframe COBOL".  Your frequent
>references to ASCII lead me to believe you are writing mainly from the PC
>perspective

No. I worked on Huge Iron at IBM Global Services two years ago.

>Feedback to Part 1.
>
>You have the comment "*>local variables" next to working-storage section. 
>Most of the literature on COBOL faults it for having global variables.  MC
>also has a local-storage section.

The explanation is semantic interpretation. My "local" meant allocated within
the program's memory. LOCAL-STORAGE means 'on the stack', which enables a
program to be reusable and reentrant. Yes, finally, mainframes do have true
stacks supported by hardware. Local variables in the context of a function,
either local or external, mean visible only to that function. 

>On MC there are three flavors of pointer: pointer, procedure-pointer and
>function-pointer.  procedure-pointer is preferred for COBOL-to-COBOL calls
>and for calls to Language Environment callable services.  function-pointers
>are used to communicate with c/c++ or java native interface.

Same on PC. I chose to omit procedure-pointer and function-pointer because
they're not very useful ,, unless you're writing a text editor with configurable
key bindings. 

>On transforming a conditional to it's negative you might mention that you
>are using DeMorgans Laws. 

Very good! I developed them independently, then was deflated to learn DeMorgan
got there first, 100 years earlier.  :(

>Starting at this point some of your ifs are missing end-if.

They are not complete COBOL statements, just instructional snippets. I didn't
want to clutter them with excessive verbiage. 

>You example of calling a local function was confusing to me. First you say
>global defeats the purpose but then your example uses global and on the
>very parameter that is being passed. 

I had the same thought. I advise against global, yet offer an example of how to
use it. I'll delete the example. 

>You might want to explain the difference between call by reference and call by
value at this point.

All calls to COBOL programs should be by reference (the default). I use by value
only when required by C functions. Your point is valid, I should explain the
difference. 

>Feedback to Part 2.
>
>Change "files thet open for update but never update" to "files that are
>opened for update but are never updated".  You might want to run spell
>check.

Thanks for the catch.

>Your demo program does not always follow your best practices.  The 05 level
>above your 88 levels in the first structure has a name, but in part 1 you
>advised against this. You also perform paragraphs but in part one you
>called this weak.

I gave it a name so I could move file-status to pf-return-code. The alternative
would have been writing 88s under file-status matched to the same values under
pf-return-code. See the discussion in this newsgroup about ambiguous file-status
values. 'File not found', one of the most common, has no standard value. It's
different on every compiler. 

Performing paragraphs is weak. I continue doing it out of habit, because it was
the norm for 40 years of COBOL's history. But more and more, I write calls to
local functions in place of performs. 
 
Thanks for the critique. 

Robert
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Best Practices - Cobol. Part 1. Seeking feedback

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-02-11T18:17:00+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b2a131$ior$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<3e441e82.170744776@news.optonline.net> <01c2d156$08ec6d20$89ccf943@chottel> <3e4876ad.199935517@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

> 'File not found', one of the most common, has no standard value. 

You seem to be incredibly resistent to assimilating information provided to 
you.

  ANSI'85 does have a standard file status code for 'File not Found'.

  Actually it has 2: 05 for optional file not found
                     35 for non-optional file not found

  All ANS'85 compilers will return this file status unless instructed 
  to return non-standard, pre'85 status codes.

How hard is this to understand ?
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3e4876ad.199935517@news.optonline.net...

> Yes, finally, mainframes do have true
> stacks supported by hardware. Local variables in the context of a
function,
> either local or external, mean visible only to that function.

Finally?  *FINALLY?*

Have you truly never heard of the Burroughs B5000/B5500/B5700 (introduced to
the market in 1961) or its successors, the Burroughs Large System and the
Unisys A Series among them?  These machines are still in production and
still actively and successfully marketed by Unisys.  Moreover, as I
understand it, Unisys sold more ClearPath HMP NX and LX series systems, the
current edition of this design, in 2002 than in any year since the
architecture was introduced, so it's not exactly riding off into the sunset
like the GE 635 and the Honeywell 200.  And it's also my understanding that
this architecture represents the largest range of upward object code
compatibility in the industry among "enterprise servers", and has for the
better part of twenty years at least.

The "mainframes" I work with have had hardware stacks (and virtual memory,
and system software written exclusively in higher-level languages, by the
way) for over forty years now!

    -Chuck Stevens
```
